# Data Literacy Exploring And Describing Data In An Ai World

> Data analysis isn’t just for specialists who need to make sense of massive datasets. Decision-makers in every industry can benefit from a basic understanding of the goals and concepts of applied data analysis. In this course, Barton Poulson focuses on the fundamentals of data fluency, or the ability to work with data to extract insights and determine your next steps. Barton shows how exploring dat

### 1. Think with Data

>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=69)** One method for describing these different levels is called the Common European Framework of Reference for Languages, or the CEFR.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=78)** This system gives three levels of linguistic ability.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=82)** The first one is basic user, and that means that you are able to do familiar expressions and basic phrases to meet very basic needs.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=91)** You don't actually have to read out of the book necessarily.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=93)** You know how to say, where am I going, where's the bathroom, and so on and so forth.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=98)** It can get you through.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=99)** It's awkward, it's difficult, but it works.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=102)** The next level is what they call the independent user, where you're able to have common conversations, as well as discussions in the technical field.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=110)** So for instance, maybe you're going to give a talk at a conference or meet with clients in another country, you can then conduct the conversation, but it's probably going to be on the stuff that you practiced.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=121)** And then the third one, according to the CEFR, is the proficient user.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=125)** This is a person who can communicate spontaneously and with a great degree of nuance on complex topics and basically anything.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=133)** So that's the native speaker.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=135)** Now, the CEFR breaks each of these down into further categories, but it gives an idea of the different levels of communication ability or linguistic fluency.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=145)** And this model can be adapted to work with data too.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=149)** So for instance, this is purely my own list, but I think it helps frame the conversation about data in fluency.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=155)** The first level is the ability to work with everyday data.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=158)** That's where you're simply trying to see common patterns and events, may not even be calculating anything.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=164)** The next level is data within a profession.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=168)** This is where you conduct data analysis as part of a larger, but non-data focused responsibility.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=176)** So for instance, you might be doing marketing analytics.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=178)** You have to work with data, but that's not your job.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=181)** Or you are a professional or academic researcher in a topical domain, you have to be able to speak the language, but it's not what your job is about.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=190)** And then the third one, I'm calling data as a profession.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=194)** This is for people who work extensively and daily with complicated data as their main professional role.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=202)** Okay, so those are three different levels.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=205)** People who maybe are even using data just intuitively, people who use it as a tool, and people for whom that is what they do.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=213)** Now, I also like to think you can simplify these a little bit more into two word phrases.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=219)** The first one is everyday data.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=220)** We're talking about the ability to work with that is data literacy.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=223)** You can read the data, you know what it means, and you can get what you need out of it.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=228)** Data within a profession.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=230)** I like to call that data fluency, which is the name of this course.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=233)** And part of that's because I'm going to be using elements of data literacy and data fluency throughout this entire course.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=238)** And the third step, data as a profession, maybe you call that one data science.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=243)** Again, these are my own terms, but they're a framework for thinking about the different responsibilities or different levels.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=249)** Not everybody needs to be a data scientist, not even everybody needs to know how to run an aggression model, being able to make a bar chart and a line chart may be sufficient for a huge number of responsibilities in the everyday data or data literacy approach.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=263)** And what that gets to is that there are terms that you may be familiar with.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=267)** There's data literacy, which I just mentioned, and data fluency.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=271)** There's also the terms quantitative or mathematical literacy, or maybe you're familiar with the term numeracy, which actually comes from a 1989 book by John Allen Paulos, which is called, "Innumeracy or Mathematical Illiteracy and its Consequences."
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=285)** But all of these refer basically to the ability to work with numbers, to work with data as a way of getting meaning and making decisions.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=293)** So I'm going to be using these terms basically interchangeably.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=297)** I know they're not exactly the same, but they have enough in common for us to use them productively.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=303)** And with that out of the way, let me tell you what we're trying to accomplish in this course, or more precisely, what we're not trying to accomplish.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=310)** This is what we are not doing.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=312)** We're not trying to turn you into a data professional and, unless you're spending 100% of your time in data science, you don't need to be able to write your own code for neural networks, and you don't need to be able to find cube roots in your head.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=324)** Besides, despite how often that kind of mental math shows up in the movies to indicate mathematical ability, it's really a separate, unrelated skill to thinking with data as any mathematician will tell you.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=336)** So we're not going to do that.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=339)** Instead, we're going to focus on the following things.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=343)** We're going to see how you can learn when you can use data to answer questions.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=347)** Data is helpful for an enormous number of things, but not everything.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=352)** Second, we're going to talk a little bit about how to frame questions so that they can be answered with data.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=357)** And we're going to present a small number of concepts and common methods that can help you find the answers to the questions that you want from the data that you have.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=368)** And finally, how to interpret those analyses and apply those answers to the questions that you had in the first place.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=375)** We're being very practical.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=377)** It's a pragmatic exercise.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=378)** Our goal is to use data to answer common questions without necessarily requiring a full on data science training.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/the-meaning-of-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=386)** Again, data literacy and data fluency and the everyday elements of data for the vast majority of people are what we're focusing on, and trying to find a way to get direction and insight as quickly and as easily as possible.

#### Data fluency is for everyone
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When people think about data, the image that frequently pops up is usually of a programmer working at some tech company in the big city.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=8)** But the fact is that pretty much everybody everywhere can benefit from data, and so that means that data fluency is important for everybody.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=18)** And when I talk about data fluency, what I'm referring to is this, thinking clearly about what's happening around you, and what you're doing.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=27)** Now, that's not necessarily something people think of when they think of data, but that's the goal of the entire procedure.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=34)** And keep in mind, even though you're looking around, and you're trying to see what's happening, you can still be creative.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=41)** You think creatively.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=42)** You can think audaciously, and take bold moves for your organization but it means you still need to be connected with reality.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=50)** In fact, that's what the data does.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=52)** The data keeps you honest.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=53)** You have to recognize what actually is happening, and it keeps you connected to reality even as you try to make bold plans for the future.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, this reminds me of an exercise that a corporate consultant I knew would do.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=67)** You'd go to an organization, you'd get up on the whiteboard, and you would ask them to write down two lists.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=72)** The first one was what's working?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=74)** Meaning what is helping to produce profitability or bring in new customers or what's making our organization work?
>

>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=98)** And then he would also ask, "What's not working?

>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=120)** And so you would had these two lists.

>

>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-is-for-everyone?u=76281980&t=128)** He says, "Can you prove it?

> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-in-practice?u=76281980&t=394)** And all of these are examples that data fluency can help people in any walk of life so they can see what's going on around them and find ways to answer questions that matter to them.

> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-in-practice?u=76281980&t=406)** Data and data fluency aren't niche topics for specialists.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-fluency-in-practice?u=76281980&t=409)** Rather, the democratization of data and data fluency can help everyone, which I hope to make abundantly clear in this course.

#### Thinking about causes

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're working in your business, you're trying to produce particular outcomes, you are trying to bring about certain effects.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=8)** That means you need to think about the causal relationship between what you do, be it an ad campaign, be it pricing, be it audience segmentation, and how those can have a a direct causal effect on the desired outcome.

> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=183)** Yeah, but maybe they would've made a lot more money had they not enrolled in the program.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=187)** And so it's important to consider what could have happened otherwise.

> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=191)** Again, theoretically, it's an impossible question, but you can look at similar cases and try to say what might have happened there.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=199)** You also have to watch out for what are called logical fallacies.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=202)** And these are things that throw you off when you're trying to reach a conclusion.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=207)** You have to look for preexisting differences.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=209)** You have to look at things like self-selection.
>

>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=222)** Well maybe they were in better health beforehand.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=225)** And you also have to look at things like history effects.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=228)** Is there something very significant that happened that affected the industry, affected outcomes separate from what you were doing?
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=236)** Also issues of attrition, maybe people drop out.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=239)** And if you ignore the dropouts, you can end up with something that looks really rosy when you're done.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=244)** But it turns out it's because all the people for whom things would not have worked out, they left.

>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=255)** These aren't really data, but they're about looking at the context in which you gather the data.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=260)** And that's going to be an important part, even if you're not actually gathering numbers.

>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=272)** Are there other things that could explain the pattern?
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=275)** Any of those will be important helping you get useful insights and useful direction out of your data as you're trying to decide what to do in your own life and your personal, professional, and social work.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/thinking-about-causes?u=76281980&t=286)** Data can help you do that, as long as you keep these issues of context and interpretation in mind.

> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=155)** Now what's happening in all three of these, with the musicians, with the books, and with the cars, is that in each case, people have made an informed decision based on the data.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=166)** Basically they're following the money, or they're following the audience, or they're following their bliss and the bliss of their audience to make decisions about what they should market, how they should sell it, and what's the most productive means.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=180)** It's actually not a magical formula. It's not hard to do.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=185)** So for instance, you can download this spreadsheet that I've created.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=189)** It's just called Decision-Making Spreadsheet.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=191)** And all you need to do is write down a few different options, a few different ways that you can make money, or a few different projects that you want to spend time on.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=199)** And you put those down under options 1, 2, 3, and 4.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=203)** And then I've got a few columns here where I put down the things that were important to me in evaluating possibilities, the level of income, how fulfilling it was, the ability for networking with other people that I want to know, and creativity.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=215)** And all of those things, you just rate them on a zero to 10 scale where 10 indicates a good outcome.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=222)** And then what the spreadsheet does is it automatically averages those, and you get a score there at the end.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=227)** Or if some of these attributes are more important to you than others, you can give them weights.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=231)** That's what's on the bottom part where I put 30%, 15%, 15%.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=235)** You can change those weights, and the spreadsheet will automatically multiply the numbers above by those weights and then create a weighted score at the end.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=242)** Let me give you an example of how this works in practice.

> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-driven-decision-making?u=76281980&t=258)** So I first put in those four options right here, and I say they could be that they join a group practice, or they start their own solo office practice, or they do online training through videos of some kind, or they maybe go work for a company and have a corporate practice.
>

#### ROI and the 80/20 rule for data fluency
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] You may be familiar with the concept of ROI or Return On Investment.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=6)** The basic idea here is how profitable is your product or service?
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=10)** What are you getting out of it for what you put into it?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=14)** Now, when you're talking about sales, you're talking about the revenue divided by the cost.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=18)** And when it comes to data, it turns out there's always a really good ROI.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=24)** I was talking recently with a consultant who said for his clients, every dollar they spent in analytics translated to at least $5 in additional profits.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=33)** You're going to have a very hard time getting a five to one or a 10 to one ratio on anything else.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=38)** So this is a great place to start.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=40)** But in general, the idea here is when you do an ROI calculation, and it allows you to choose the products or the services or the things you're working on that give you the best ROI.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=53)** And it's not difficult to do.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=55)** It turns out that when you're doing ROI, there's a principle that applies.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=60)** It comes up pretty frequently.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=61)** And people know it colloquially as the 80/20 rule.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=66)** Now this comes from an Italian economist, Vilfredo Pareto in the 19th century, and it's also known as the Pareto principle.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=75)** The 80/20 and the Pareto principle are basically the same thing.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=78)** And what Pareto found in his research was that 80% of the land in Italy was owned by 20% of the population.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=87)** So ownership was not evenly distributed, 80% of the land owned by 20% of the population.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=93)** Well, people have found that you often have this kind of disproportionate outcome in a lot of other situations.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=100)** So for instance, it's pretty common that people get 80% of their output from 20% of their input.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/roi-and-the-80-20-rule-for-data-fluency?u=76281980&t=106)** By the way, the two numbers don't have to add up to 100.
>

> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=264)** You simply show them for instance a whole bunch of photos and say, this is this person.

> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=268)** This is this person.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=269)** And it figures out on its own what it needs to look at.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=272)** And the same thing happens with recommendation engines, the algorithm figures out on its own what's going to be most productive based on the data that it has.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=280)** You don't have to tell it.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=281)** And so it means that sometimes you got to let go.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=284)** You got to let go of trying to micromanage everything that the computer does and let it make its own decisions based on its own rules and its observations.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=293)** Another interesting one from the field of data science has to do with Bayes' Theorem now.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=298)** So Bayes' Theorem is a method, it's a formula for incorporating prior data or even subjective beliefs into probability calculations.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=307)** And Bayesian analysis has been responsible for some really amazing developments.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=312)** But the idea here is it never takes the data at face value.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=315)** It incorporates other information, including really, before you even did the analysis what did you think was the likelihood of this being true?
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=324)** That's called a subjective prior and it's legitimate thing to incorporate.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=329)** And the idea here is you're not driven just by the data but there are other things that go into that conglomeration of decision making.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=337)** Now this gets us to one last thing here.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=339)** The idea is, you know, don't crush it.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=341)** When you're doing data work you run the risk of something called overfitting and overfitting is a major risk, a problem in statistical analysis and machine learning.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=352)** This is when your analysis is too closely tied to the current data and you're trying to match every tiny little variation in the current dataset.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=361)** You'll end up with a good prediction for that dataset, but that's not the data you're interested in.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=365)** You're interested in what other people are going to do in the future.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=369)** And the problem is when you create an analysis that is overfitted, it simply doesn't generalize well to new times, new places, new products, new people.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=379)** And it turns out that loosening your analytical grip creates solutions typically that generalize better to new situations.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=387)** And so you're not trying to nail everything down right here, right now, but you're trying to get the principles that are going to work well in other situations.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=396)** The moral of all of the this is that you want to use data whenever possible.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/putting-data-in-context?u=76281980&t=401)** Data's going to help, but use your brain always in every single situation and taken together, using the data that you have and your own common sense, you're going to make better high quality decisions than you could make using either method on it's own.

### 2. Prepare Data

#### Data ethics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The information you have about your business, about your customers and their online connections, about the people who see your social media, the data that you have is an incredible resource.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=11)** You can get some amazing value out of your data, but first you need to learn to treat your data, and the people who gave it to you with respect and give it the care it deserves.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=22)** These elements of data ethics are the foundation of the practice of data and data fluency that we're talking about.

>

>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=65)** If people don't trust you, they're either not going to give you the information, or they may give you bad information, which is so much worse than no information at all.

>

>

>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=96)** It's the nice thing to do and people appreciate that.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, I want to mention some of the important laws and regulations that affect data.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=106)** Number one and biggest for people who work with data is the European Union's General Data Protection regulation or GDPR.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=115)** Now, this has been effect since 2018 and what this law does is it affects how data can be gathered, what can be done with it, what peoples rights to privacy and their own data are and so on.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=127)** And I'll mention that since the UK's departure from the European Union, they've had their own essentially identical law, the UKGDPR since 2021.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=138)** Here in the United States the California Consumer Privacy Act, or CCPA is a law that's also covers many of the same issues, it's been enforced since 2020 then amended and expanded with the California Privacy Rights Act which takes effect in 2023.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=155)** Also, you may be a member of a professional organization, and they have their own regulations that may for instance affect licensure or be a member in good standing, and you need to abide by those and how they affect data, and then maybe there's a particular company or organization you work for and they may have their own policies about how data is treated.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=175)** And so you can go from an international regulation to a state regulation, to a professional regulation, to a organizational regulation, and really all of them are going to tell you to do some of the exact same things and so let's talk about what some of those things are.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=191)** Number one, the idea is that whenever you get data you need to have people's consent and that means people need to give you their permission to use their data, to gather their data, to process it, to do something with it.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=203)** People need to know what you're getting into and they need to give you their permission.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=207)** Now, closely to add to that is the issue of informed consent.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=211)** People need to know exactly what it is that they're agreeing to.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=215)** You don't necessarily need to tell them absolutely every single detail, but enough that they know if they want this to happen or not.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=224)** And then the third element is that their consent needs to be not only informed, but voluntary.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=230)** That means that consent needs to be given freely and not coerced and the problem is coercion comes in many different ways.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=237)** If a person needs to use a particular online service for example, in order to do their business and in order to use that online service, you have to give away some of your data that is potentially a form of coercion.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=250)** And another element to remember here is that consent can be revoked.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=254)** If it's some later data, a person decides they no longer want to share their data with you, regulations like the GDPR make it possible for them to say you can't have my data, you got to delete it, you got to give it to me, I was never a part of that.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=266)** So, consent where people give permission, inform they know what they're getting into and voluntary where it's done freely are some of the most important and foundational elements of the ethical gathering and use of data.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=280)** And if you do these things, they build a relationship.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=284)** It's a mutual agreement about data and how you are going to provide some value for the person, they're going to provide some value for you and you build that connection, it's a good relationship.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=295)** On the other hand there are other things that can break the relationship.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=298)** Like for example, violations of anonymity.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=303)** Data is often not always, but often meant to be anonymous, so that people cannot be identified by the data, or their data cannot be linked with them.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=312)** There have been some surprising violations of anonymity and I'll show you how this is actually a little more complicated these days than it used to be.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=320)** But the other elements is confidentiality, and the idea here is that people's data needs to be kept private and not shared with anyone without their consent.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=330)** And we have had some huge violations of this in recent years, and you can see the fallout both legal and the PR fallout from those.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=340)** And those can damage the relationship that an organization is supposed to be setting up with the people that they are ideally serving.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=347)** So let me talk about anonymity for just a moment.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=351)** Anonymity used to be really easy, if you're giving somebody a paper questionnaire don't put your name on it and that kind of kept it anonymous, but now with a digital age where you are gathering huge amounts of data and you're keeping all of it and you're connecting all of it it's really easy to know who someone is, even when they don't put their names down.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=370)** For example, if you can get just four observations of a person's location, the latitude and longitude, and the time that they were there, you have a very high probability of knowing exactly who they are.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=381)** And since cell phones track that information by default, you potentially have a very serious threat to anonymity if you don't specifically address it.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=390)** And then confidentiality, when companies share private information without people's explicit consent, by sharing with another company or by selling it that's like gossiping behind someone's back and obviously people get very, very upset about it.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=403)** And that's one of the major motivations for regulations like the GDPR.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=409)** And the consequences of all of this can be pretty serious.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=412)** Laws like the GDPR get passed to reduce these risks, so the failure to self-regulate brings on external regulation.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=420)** Also these violations damage trust and there's a lot of people now who are very unhappy with online companies, and try to find ways not to do business with them 'cause they feel that they have been mistreated in the past.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=432)** It also means that people may refuse to share data, or they may give bad data.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=436)** And like I said, bad data is worse than no data at all, and so it creates a more difficult environment in which to operate.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=445)** The moral of all of this is pretty simple.
>
> **[7:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-ethics?u=76281980&t=448)** When it comes to working with data you need to treat people with respect, and if you can do that, their data will give you true value.

> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-in-house-data?u=76281980&t=265)** That way you're actually going to have even more value for your in-house data and get more insight from what you already have, which does an enormous amount in terms of saving you time and getting you moving quickly.

#### Use open data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you're trying to get answers to things that matter to you and you don't already have the data sitting around, you don't have to reinvent the wheel.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=8)** An amazing amount of data's out there, just waiting for you, ready to go and it's what's called open data.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=15)** Now, the idea with open data is this, it's data that is free, it's easily accessible and it's downloadable in convenient forms like for instance, a CSV or comma separated values which is a common form of spreadsheet data, but also may be ready to go into Python or R or into your SQL database or whatever you have.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=35)** It's there and it's ready to go.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=38)** Now there's several advantages to using open data.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=42)** Many of these projects may have large samples, stuff that you could never do on your own.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=47)** They also may have panels over time.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=49)** What that means is they've gone back to the same people every year to see how things are changing.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=56)** That'd be nearly impossible for any private organization or individual to do on their own.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=62)** The data may also be well prepared and well documented.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-open-data?u=76281980&t=67)** And so these are some potential advantages of open data and there are a lot of different sources, doors you can go through to get your data.
>

> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/gather-new-data?u=76281980&t=234)** Now that's important 'cuz I come from an academic background, and there are surveys I've seen that have 850 questions on them.

#### Use third-party data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-third-party-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-third-party-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you don't already have the data that you need and if you can't find any of it in the open data repositories and if you don't have the time or ability to gather the data yourself, there's always the option of just buying the data.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-third-party-data?u=76281980&t=15)** Now, the way you do that usually is by going through what's called a data broker, and data brokers are companies that gather data from a lot of different sources and process it in many different ways and then make it available to you for a fee.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/use-third-party-data?u=76281980&t=30)** And some of the services they provide can include scoring the data, making inferences.

> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=97)** Were there time pressures for people?
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=99)** Also, what questions were asked?
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=101)** Do you know the exact text to which people were responding?
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=105)** 'Cause if you know anything about, say, for instance, political polls, it's very easy to spin the responses that people give by subtly leading them with selective words.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=114)** So, you need to know the exact text.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=117)** And then there's also the question about labels and metadata.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=120)** Do you actually know what all the values in the data mean?
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=123)** And that's going to be the foundational elements of being able to make something sensible out of the data.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=131)** Also, you need to know how the data was processed once it was gathered.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=135)** For instance, did they combine any categories?
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=138)** Are there any unusual combination of scores that maybe should be separated?
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=143)** A very important one is whether there are any outliers or other anomalies in the data, because outliers exert a massive influence on any analysis and they can very easily mislead you.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=156)** Also, anomalies may tell you that there was something wrong with the sampling process or there was something wrong with the measurement process or that you're onto something new and different.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=165)** And then there's the issue of missing data.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=168)** Sometimes what's not there can be more telling than what is there.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=173)** Is it at random?
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=175)** Is it meaningful?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=176)** Who, for instance, didn't reply to certain questions, or who dropped out partway through, or who didn't start at all?
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=182)** Because that's going to give you biases in your data and you're going to need to know what is missing.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=188)** And can you tell that somebody was missing, or were they simply completely removed from the dataset so you never know that it was there?
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=195)** Also, when people are answering questions, you need to know about things like, did they get bored because it was long or did they get overwhelmed because it was so demanding, and then they start giving essentially random data?
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=207)** Were they confused?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=208)** Some questions are inherently confusing, and I'll talk about that in another video talking about the meaning of the questions.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=214)** Also, do you have to worry about things like attrition and people selectively dropping out of the study partway through or not answering questions or simply not wanting to have anything to do with it anymore?
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=225)** And another thing that pops up even in very strange circumstances is the effect of social desirability.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=230)** People want to give the right answers even when it's an anonymous study that'll have no impact on them.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=235)** They still want to feel good about themselves while they're writing, so they may tell you what they think you want to hear as opposed to accurate data which you really need to work with.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=244)** I'm not saying that all of these happen in every single data set, but they are questions that you need to ask when looking at your data, because they're going to dramatically affect your ability to interpret the data and to get useful insights out of it.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=258)** Really, what it all means is the flip side of the garbage in, garbage out.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=261)** If you have quality data going in, then you have at least the possibility of quality insights coming out of your analysis.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-quality-of-data?u=76281980&t=270)** Again, the idea here is that nothing's perfect, but if you consider these elements when assessing the quality of your data and hopefully have answers for them, then you can get more insight out of your data and you can also make a more persuasive argument to support your decisions based on your analysis of your data.

>

>

>

>

>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=271)** But any of them compromise, again, the ability to accurately generalize your responses from the data that you currently have to other new situations.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=281)** Generalizability will also suffer depending on what you measured and how it relates to your intended outcome or criterion.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=289)** For instance, everybody knows that the number of people who buy gym memberships because they want to get fit and they intend to exercise is very different from the number of people who actually show up on a regular basis.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=300)** In psychology, it's common to talk about the attitude-behavior discrepancy.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=304)** Attitudes are, how do people feel about your business and what do they say that their intentions are?
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=311)** And that's a little different thing from the actual behaviors.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=315)** What do people actually do in reality in relation to your business?
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=318)** What are their actions and their behaviors?
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=320)** And these can be measured both through self-report, self-reported attitudes and self-reported behaviors where people tell you what they did, or it can be measured through intentions.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=329)** For instance, I'm going to register for this meetup group versus actually showing up.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=334)** Everybody knows that there's a big gap between those two different things.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=338)** And those are the difference between attitudes and behaviors.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=341)** If you're trying to predict attitudes, then you need to measure attitudes.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=344)** But if you're trying to predict behaviors, you usually want to make sure that you are measuring behaviors in your original data set 'cause you can't really generalize from attitudes to behaviors because there's so much slippage between the two of them.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=355)** And so, what can you do about these kinds of generalizability problems?
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=359)** What are the solutions?
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=360)** Well, the single most important thing is to match your measurement to your outcome.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=364)** Again, if you're trying to predict the behavior of a particular group of people, make sure they're the ones that you measure.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=370)** That will give you the easiest carryover from your original data set to your conclusions.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=377)** Also, be aware of environmental changes, things that are happening in the economic environment, the educational environment, businesses opening and closing.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=385)** Those will all affect your results.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=387)** But the single most important thing is to experiment.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=390)** If you do it again and again, try different variations and see how well your results generalize from one trial to another, then you're going to have the best basis for generalizing your results.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=404)** A single shot is rarely sufficient.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=407)** It's rarely persuasive, it rarely generalizes well.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-generalizability-of-data?u=76281980&t=410)** But many variations are going to give you a much better grasp on what's happening with the people that you're trying to understand and the ability that you have to better serve them.

#### Assess the ambiguities in data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=9)** This can come from several different sources one of which is measuring disagreement.

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=97)** Is it that they love opera, but they don't love the costuming.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=99)** Is it they love the costuming, but they don't love opera?
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=101)** Or is it they don't like either one?
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=103)** And so, a doubled or a compound question is really a bad thing 'cause you don't know what people are responding to.

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=111)** In this case, of course, you're going to have to split it up into two questions, try to get it down to a more granular level.

> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=126)** If a person puts neutral it could mean that they neither agree nor disagree that they're indifferent, you know, take it or leave, it doesn't mean much to me.

> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=134)** But it could also mean that they love some parts of their job and hate others, that they are ambivalent, that they are harboring really a high tension situation.
