> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=146)** Indifference and ambivalence are very psychologically different.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=150)** And what this means is you're going to have to try to break things down to avoid the possibility of ambivalence.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=156)** Now, that means you usually have to ask more questions and asking more questions creates its own problems.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=160)** So you're going to have to be very focused about what you're trying to do in the first place.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=165)** And, of course, there are other ambiguities, and confusions in language.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=169)** Say, for instance, you're working in data science and you're building a machine learning model to process the text that people have in their social media posts.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=177)** Well, machine learning models applied to natural language like that have trouble with things that people understand pretty well.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=184)** Things like irony, and sarcasm, and slang.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=186)** Things like double entendres, and oxymorons, and misspellings.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=190)** And so, it becomes a little more difficult.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=193)** You have to say exactly how your algorithm dealt with each of these potential situations.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=199)** That's the operational definition.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=200)** It gets you back to the ability to interpret your information.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=204)** Now, the idea is you're going to build a computer that's going to do everything perfectly, but you need to remember that even human raters have trouble agreeing on classifying the positive and negative valence, or emotional elements of natural language.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=217)** So you can't expect computers to be more consistent than humans, you're going to have to understand that there's a lot of ambiguity and a lot of slippage, again, in how people interpret these things.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=227)** And that is going to introduce sort of this irreducible element in the interpretability of your results because there is fundamental differences in the way that humans interpret these things.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=240)** The moral of all of this is pretty simple, prepare carefully and it also means prompt for confirmation of meaning.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=249)** You want to make sure you understand the people who are giving you data.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/assess-the-ambiguities-in-data?u=76281980&t=252)** So check with them, make sure that the two of you are on the same page, and then you'll be able to take actions that better match the needs, the understandings, and the desires of the people you're serving.

#### Code text
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the rules of thumb in the data world is that data preparation typically accounts for 80% of the time in the data project, you got to spend a lot of time to get it ready before you can do the analysis, and one of the important parts of getting data prepared is taking text open-ended responses, that's things like tweets and social media posts, and open-ended questions, and putting it into a format that's going to be easy to analyze for the computer, and normally that means turning it into numbers.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=34)** So we're going to take text and turn it to numbers.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=36)** Now, it's important to start with text because open text is flexible, so if you're going to give a questionnaire you have, put your comment here or you take something like a comment that a person makes when they buy something on Amazon.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=48)** Open text is really flexible and it gives you a really good insight sometimes into what people are thinking, because it's not restricted.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=57)** The problem is that text is hard to explore, to summarize, and to model without actually reading every single piece of it.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=64)** That's fine if you've got 50, but if you've got 50,000, that's going to be a different issue.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=69)** And the thing here is that numbers are often easier because it's really easy to batch them and to treat them as groups and get a summary of them.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=79)** So the point of that is you want to convert text to numbers when it makes sense.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=85)** When you're ready to give up some of the nuance of the data in order for speed, and interpretability, and the ability to manipulate.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=93)** So let's start by talking about one method, that's just simple coding.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=98)** Now, sometimes when people talk about open text they talk about qualitative data, and I'm not talking about that right now.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=105)** What I'm doing here is, again, I'm losing nuance, we're going to be losing some of the value in the data, but it's a trade off, it's an informed decision, it's for the sake of speed, and for the sake of simplicity and analysis.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=120)** And what that often means is you're reducing stuff all the way down to yes or no, using ones and zeros.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=126)** So again, this isn't qualitative data, to me qualitative data is very rich, rigorous research, getting up to ethnography.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=134)** We're just counting yeses and noes, and we're going to boil things down to that level.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=139)** Now, when you do this and you create these variables with 1s and 0s, they go by several different names.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=146)** I personally call them indicator variables, where the 1 indicates the presence of something.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=152)** They're also called dichotomous variables, because dichotomy means to split it into two pieces, and you're doing that by splitting it into the yeses and the noes.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=162)** And these research world they're often called dummy variables for reasons that are not clear to me, I don't like that term, but you'll hear about dummy variables, for instance, in regression.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=172)** In the computer programming world they're also called Boolean variables for George Bool, who did a lot about logic and the true and the false.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=182)** By the way, they're also called binary variables because they take two possible values.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=188)** Now, heres some of the things that you need to consider.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=190)** First off is, are you looking at something that is relatively objective and relatively subjective?
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=198)** You know, it's really easy to code for, quote unquote objective characteristics, like did a person mention that they are employed?
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=206)** Or did they say yes, I like this?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=208)** Or do they own this item?
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=211)** Those things are easy.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=212)** You can get a hundred people to look at them and a hundred people will agree on what's going on there.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=217)** The subjective things are a lot harder, say for instance, are they frustrated with what's going on here?
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=223)** Well, that depends a lot on the wording.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=226)** And so it's just important to remember you can still do subjective, it's going to be just a little bit harder than the objective, and I'm putting both of those in quotes because they are fuzzy categories.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=238)** One of the important things is when you do this kind of indicator coding is that you deal with categories that are mutually exclusive.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=245)** The point here is that your answers need to fall into only one value at a time.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=250)** It needs to be very clear that you're saying like is this person right-handed or are they lefthanded?
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=256)** And you have the problem of people who are ambidextrous.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=258)** So you need to be able to say are they able to do stuff with the right hand?
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=261)** And then separately, are they able to do stuff with the left hand?
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=265)** So you're creating categories where you're going to have one value at a time.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=270)** Also, as I just mentioned, you're probably going to have to create multiple variables, and what that means is you're going to use many indicator variables to indicate multiple categories.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=281)** So say for instance, you want to know what field a person works in, and you've got that information from an open-ended response, You're going to have to put down a lot of different fields that put down the general categories of employment, so you can put them in one or the other.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=298)** Now here's the actual process, how you go about doing this?
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=303)** First off, you want to get multiple people to read the text to get a sense of the themes to code.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=309)** What's in there?
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=310)** What's the universe of potential things that we're dealing with?
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=314)** And then together you can agree on a list of codes.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=317)** These are the things that we're going to be looking for, that we're going to mark them as yes or no, and you can look literally put them on little sticky notes and put them on the wall, so people remember this is what we're coding for.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=329)** And then you have the people work independently and they each code the text independently.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=334)** So they have these different variables they're going to mark, whether this theme occurred yes or no, and they do it separately.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=341)** That way you can test for what's called a inter-rater reliability, and when people code things differently you can discuss the differences and hopefully resolve them, and get something that you all feel comfortable with.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=353)** So that's the general outline of the process.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=357)** Now, in terms of the actual codes it's best to use 1 and 0, and I have to mention this because there's a lot of programs that by default use 1 and 2 where one is the first answer, two is the second answer, and a lot of people do that when they're putting stuff on surveys.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=373)** That's super confusing because it's really hard to remember which one's the 1 and which one's the 2.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=378)** But if you use the 1 and 0 there's a strong practice there, where 1 represents true and 0 represents false in programming, that's when it's called a Boolean variable.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=392)** And then what you do is you name the variable after the value for 1, so, if you want to put down employed that would be the name of the variable, and 1 would mean true, they are employed, and zero would mean false, so they're not employed.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=407)** That's the easy way to do it and you create many variables where each of them you name it after what the true value the 1's going to be, and then it's really easy to remember.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=418)** There are other statistical advantages to using the 1s and 0s, if you're going to do correlations or regressions then they work really simply.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=426)** So use 1 and 0, not 1 and 2.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=430)** All right, the next step here is to take your data set and fill in the 0s, so you're going to put in 1s, you're going to mark the 1s, probably do this in a spreadsheet, and then use find and replace to fill in the remaining 0s, and then after that, you're going to make sure that you use k-1 categories when you're doing modeling.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=452)** Now, this one's a little more advanced.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=454)** If you're going to be doing what's called linear regression, you use the indicator variables, let's say you have five different categories that you're worried about.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=464)** Then you have five variables, k indicator variables, so five of them, to model an outcome.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=469)** But when you do the regression, you don't use all of them, you use one less, you use four of the five, because if you use all five of them then they completely determine each other and that gives you something called multicollinearity, which in the analytical world is a very bad thing.
>
> **[8:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=486)** So, when you have these categories you use 1 as the baseline and then they use the others to represent changes from the baseline.
>
> **[8:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=495)** Let me show you how this actually works.
>
> **[8:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=497)** I've done a little tiny sample of this in Excel.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=500)** What we have here is coding some text, where we ask seven people about what sorts of things they like about their job, we're getting a little feedback on their work.
>
> **[8:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=511)** By the way this is just made up.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=513)** But you see first we're going to get a feel for the kinds of things that they say.
>
> **[8:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=517)** I love the people and the flexibility, hours are good, pay is good, close to home, and so on.
>
> **[8:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=523)** Well, based on that, I can identify maybe six general themes, whether they like the people, the pay is good, the benefits, the flexible hours, work from home, and location.
>
> **[8:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=534)** And those are the things that I'm going to code for.
>
> **[8:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=536)** So I go through each person's response and if they have one of those things that they mention, I put a 1 there to indicate, for instance this first one says, I love the people, so in column C I put a 1, and then for a few beneath it, on it's actually the Excel row 5, but it's ID 4, it says good colleagues, flexible hours, so I put a 1 there also for them.
>
> **[9:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=558)** So I fill it in with all the things that people mentioned in their very brief responses, and then what I do is I just do find and replace in Excel.
>
> **[9:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=568)** Find the empty cells within that range and replace them with 0s.
>
> **[9:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=573)** And now I have a data set that I can either analyze in Excel and I can sum things up, I can also get proportions, I can do potentially modeling, I can import it into another statistical program, I've got a lot that I can do.
>
> **[9:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/code-text?u=76281980&t=587)** I've taken my open text and by intentionally simplifying it, I'm going to be able to get a lot more quantitative and analytical value out of it, a lot faster and a lot more easily.

#### Calculate sums and means
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the things you find when dealing with data is there's a lot of noise.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=4)** And one of your big problems, one of the big challenges, is to try to find the signal hidden in the noise.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=13)** And it turns out that one of the really helpful ways to do that is to average, is to sum things or average things.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=20)** Because by aggregating, it makes it easier to isolate the true signal.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=26)** Let me give you an interesting example of this is accuracy by aggregate.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=31)** And here we have a jar of jelly beans.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=34)** And maybe you've done something like this where you ask people to guess how many jelly beans are in a jar, and you're going to give a prize to whoever is most accurate.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=42)** So everybody writes down their estimate, and then they put it on a piece of paper and they put that in a bowl, and you're going to pick somebody who gets it the most accurate.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=52)** Well, it turns out that your best approach actually is to take all of the guesses that people give and then average those guesses.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=60)** So not an individual guess but the mean.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=63)** Because one of the interesting things is that the average will typically be closer to the true value than any one individual guess.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=70)** Some people tend to underestimate, some overestimate.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=74)** And if you want to know which one should you pick, the answer is pick all of them.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=78)** Average them.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=80)** It's a general rule that gives you a reasonable level of confidence that you have the best estimate based on the data you have.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=87)** Again, by averaging, you cancel out a lot of the individual noise, and you can zero in on the signal that's there in the data.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=96)** Similarly, when you're trying to measure something, when you're asking people questions about their work or their city or how they feel about the educational opportunities they have, when you're measuring them, what you're going to do is you're going to measure constructs.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=112)** Remember, a construct is a abstract thing that you're trying to measure by getting several observable characteristics by asking specific questions.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=122)** And you're going to measure constructs by asking several related items.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=126)** The reason you ask several is because no one item is a perfect measure of the thing you're looking for.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=132)** And also, some of those items, well, they'll give you values that are high, some that are low for that individual person.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=140)** And so you're trying to get past the fluctuations that are built into each item, again, to hone in on the signal.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=147)** And like the jellybean example, the average of those several items that are trying to measure a single construct typically are more accurate pictures of the construct than any one separate question.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=161)** To give an interesting example of this process, we can look at the fashion fluency scale that was developed by Joshua Brown, a psychological researcher.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=169)** The idea was to measure how much people knew about fashion.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=172)** And the way he decided to do this was by asking people about 17 different people and topics in fashion.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=179)** Now he got these topics from a reference book on fashion, and he tested them with several fashion designers.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=185)** And what he would do is he would give people these 17 items.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=189)** And you can see we got Coco Chanel.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=191)** I named my dog after her.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=192)** The Paraphernalia Boutique and so on.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=195)** And on each of these, people would say how familiar they were where they'd say, zero, never heard of it.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=200)** Don't know anything.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=201)** Up to three.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=203)** I could give an extended presentation on this topic.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=205)** I know everything about it.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=207)** And so people would rate their familiarity with each of these.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=209)** The idea is no one of these stands in for fashion as a whole, but taken together, they zero in on the signal of fashion and cancel out the noise that's around them individually.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=225)** By the way, I do want to mention this scale, the 17 items, fell into two categories, so you get two scores.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=231)** One is for knowledge of fashion history, and a second is for recognition of fashion labels.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=237)** Now to give another example of using sums and means to cancel out the noise, here is some fictional revenue data over time.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=246)** It's going from April of 2013 up through October of 2018.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=251)** And what we're getting is monthly revenues, and you can see it's very jagged.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=256)** It starts out a little low, and it goes up really high, but we've got these really big fluctuations from month to month.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=262)** And the problem is when you look at the chart, the things you notice the most are the points where it's going up and down.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=268)** That is an inherent problem of when you're looking at stuff too closely.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=274)** A way to back up is to get something called a moving average and that's going to be another way to chart it.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=280)** With a moving average, you can take a certain number of observations and average them as you go across.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=287)** Now I decided to do it with a six-month moving average.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=291)** And here's what it looks like. It's so much clearer.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=295)** Each observation on the blue line is based on the average of the six previous months.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=300)** And you can see it is so much more stable.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=303)** And the linear trend going up and up and up is so much easier to see.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=309)** Again, what we're doing is we're getting rid of a lot of the noise and the random fluctuation and focusing on the signal.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=316)** Now one other way to do it is to do this with sums.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=320)** And that's where, for instance, you count how often a person visits your site in one month or one quarter.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=325)** And you find that this is more stable than day-by-day measurements, or you can divide by days to get the average visits per year.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=331)** But simply adding up averages out the fluctuation of day to day to get a truer picture.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=338)** And I'll finish with one more thing.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=340)** I want to say something about the advantages of summing or averaging.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=344)** Number one, it makes it easier to visualize and communicate as you don't have to communicate as much stuff.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=349)** You're going to give an average instead of 10 or 100 or 100,000 scores.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=355)** It makes it easier to focus in.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=358)** Second, in the same way, it smooths out the random noise and makes it easier to find the signal, either across several questions, all at once, or a trend across time.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=368)** And what all of these do is they allow you to generalize more accurately because you're no longer distracted by the separate fluctuations, but you're able to see the overall pattern of the signal.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-sums-and-means?u=76281980&t=380)** And that's the thing that is more likely to carry over and give you better insight into the patterns in your data.

> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=231)** Not only is it an incredible investment, "El Mariachi" was deemed by the Library of Congress to be culturally, historic, or aesthetically significant, and so they added to the United States National Film Registry.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=244)** That's amazing.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=245)** And again, it's important to keep in this rate, how many dollars do you earn for each dollar that you invest, and compare that to a movie like "John Carter," which earned over $180 million, which will make you think it's a huge success, but it costs $300 million to make.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=263)** So it was a massive loss.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=265)** It was a way to lose all of your money.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=269)** Again, the point here is that it's important to look at the rate or percentage return, not just the absolute earnings.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=276)** That's where taking the value you care about, like the earnings, and dividing it either by the number of films in the series, or by the cost, can be a much, much better way of getting an impression of how good a particular investment would be.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=292)** And let me give a few other examples.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=294)** If you're selling an item, you can look at the profit per unit and see which ones are most profitable for you.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=300)** Similarly, you can look at what the problems per unit are, which ones are causing the most trouble in terms of customer returns, or complaints, or broken equipment.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=309)** And you can find ways of isolating those.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=312)** And if you're in sales, you can look at things like sales per client, or value per sale.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=316)** Any one of those is going to be a rate that's going to help you find the return on investment for the thing you're looking at.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/calculate-rates?u=76281980&t=323)** And those are going to be some of the easiest and most influential measures you can have, and you get them simply by taking one value and dividing it by another to get the per unit price.

#### AI-assisted data preparation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-assisted-data-preparation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

### 4. Explore Data

#### Bar charts
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The goal for bar charts is easy, to make a simple visualization that tells you how many observations fall into a category, or how much of some value for those same category.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=11)** And in terms of the variables you work with, you might look at something like the number of new clients versus returning clients, or the number of views for each of your products, or the profitability you have for each service, or contacts per customer, number of complaints, requests for new products or services, how many times you travel to the warehouse, or anything else that can be counted or measured and can be broken down by a group.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=36)** Now when you're showing the actual data, you have the option of showing frequencies, that's how many observations there are in that particular category.
>

> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=366)** And then actually adding text to the chart itself to explain and recommend.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=373)** And of course the thing about visualizations is they answer some questions but they always lead to some interesting ones that you want to follow up with.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=380)** For instance, with the educational data, who earns degrees and who doesn't?
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=384)** What are the consequences of not having a degree?
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=387)** What are the factors that contribute to a person getting or not getting a college degree?
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=393)** What issues of equity, access, and justice are raised?
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=396)** And of course those are things in which you would need additional data, but it lets you know that you're starting a conversation with your data.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/bar-charts?u=76281980&t=402)** You're getting a narrative, and it's something that you can expand on to get more insight and potentially more value out of your data.

>

#### Pie charts

>

> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=4)** But you know, I have to begin by acknowledging by even mentioning pie charts a lot of people are going to be turned off because pie charts have a very bad reputation in the data visualization world.

>

> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=126)** Now please note it instantly went from the number of cases, the frequencies to percentages because pie charts work best for showing percentages and it gets people's attention.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=139)** I had to do a little bit to make it easier to read by putting in the names of each categories and the percentages and getting a little more contrast and making them bigger.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=148)** So this works and you also have a variation on a pie chart that's called a donut chart.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=154)** That's where you got a big hole in the middle.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=156)** And I actually prefer donut charts if I'm going to be making them.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=160)** This time, I have the title in the middle and I've got these rings around side.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=165)** And now it's a little bit more like a line chart because people are interpreting the length of each of the arcs.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=171)** So that might actually be easier to read.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=173)** But one of the best things you can do with a pie chart or a donut chart is to simplify it even more.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=179)** And I make charts like this sometimes.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=181)** I highlight one category.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=183)** I put all the rest in gray and then right there in the middle I'll say Facebook is 32% of total subscribers.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=189)** Now it reads like a gauge and that's really easy to read.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=195)** So there's some real potential for pie charts.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=198)** But the important thing is you have to think first.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=201)** The mindful and deliberate and thoughtful use of pie charts can be a very effective tool in both exploring data and presenting it to other people.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=212)** There are potential problems.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=214)** There's a lot of them and I'll show you a few.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=216)** Interpretation can be hard and things like using 3D can dramatically mislead people when they're looking at pie charts.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=225)** The general rule here is that you really need to simplify.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=229)** You need to do things like a range in descending values.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=232)** You start at 12 o'clock, 'cause they don't all do that by default.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=236)** You need to make sure that your numbers total to a 100%.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=239)** I've seen some where they add up to something other than a 100% and it's completely confusing.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=245)** That means by the way, you need to have mutually exclusive categories.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=248)** A case falls into only one of the categories.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=252)** So that it adds up to a hundred percent.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=254)** Also, don't separate the slices, sometimes called exploding them because then it's really hard to compare them.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=259)** And just as a general rule, don't use 3D ever in a pie chart and I'll show you why.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=266)** So let's begin with a default pie chart.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=269)** This is the number of followers by social media platform.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=272)** This is the data.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=273)** It doesn't have the percentages at all but I want you to look at something here.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=276)** Take a look at this slice down here and then take a look at this slice.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=281)** And I want you to tell me which one of these is bigger and if one's bigger, by how much?
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=287)** And it turns out that's really hard to do with a pie chart.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=292)** In fact, let me move this over to the side and then let's show the same data with bar charts.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=298)** So this is the exact same data, same color, same order.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=302)** And now we're going to compare the Twitter bar with the Pinterest bar and now it's totally obvious that they're different from each other and that Twitter's bigger.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=311)** So here's the Twitter level.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=312)** Here's the Pinterest level.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=314)** And there's the size of the difference when it would be almost impossible to put that into words based on the pie chart.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=321)** So that's number one.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=323)** Pie charts, you have to be able to read angles and you have to be able to read the area of segments of a circle.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=330)** Those are operations from trigonometry and calculus.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=334)** But a bar chart, all you have to do is judge linear length and comparisons.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=339)** there are operations from arithmetic.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=342)** It's conceptually, cognitively so much easier in most situations to make comparisons using a bar chart.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=350)** Also in terms of 3D, disasters like this can happen.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=354)** I was using a program which shall remain nameless.
>

>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=360)** It put all those textures in, it angled it, it's just crazy.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=365)** So I actually had to do a little bit of work to simplify it.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=368)** So this is a 3D chart, but we're looking at it straight on and what I want you to see here, are some of the angles.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=375)** Now here's a little overlay where we've got the black line highlighting a few of the angles.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=382)** Watch what happens when we tilt it back which is something that people always do when they make a 3D chart.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=388)** When you tilt it back, it actually changes the angles.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=392)** Those black lines are no longer on the dividing lines.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=396)** In fact, the, the new black lines would have to be higher.
>
> **[6:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=398)** The angle has become shallower.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=401)** That actually influences the way that you read the angles.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=404)** Plus, very often this black rim across the bottom gets colored the same color as the slices and it gets read as data and it would make those bottom two, Instagram and Twitter, look much bigger than they should be.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=418)** And so it's very easy to misinterpret, especially if you use a 3D pie chart.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=424)** And so the idea again is that sometimes less is more.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=428)** So take your pie chart.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=430)** Here's a pretty simple one and then narrow it down, get it down to the thing you're really trying to focus on.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=437)** Here's another one where I've made a donut chart with a hole in the middle.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=440)** I've highlighted the one category I care about right now.
>

>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=449)** That makes it really easy again, to guide the eye and to give a relative value and a number to put on it.
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=458)** And that takes care of both the impressionistic utility of charts and it gives the precision.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/pie-charts?u=76281980&t=464)** And so in this particular situation, I think a pie chart or a donut chart is in fact probably the best solution for focusing on the one category and drawing attention and leading the narrative through it which after all, is one of the main purposes of data visualization in any situation.

#### Dot plots
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With a data visualization, you get to see your data for the forest, the entire collection.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=6)** But with a dot plot, it's a little different, you get to see the trees.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=10)** The point of a dot plot is actually not to summarize, but to show you every single point in your data.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, in terms of the kinds of variables, the kinds of data you can show, well, anything that is measured or scaled, sometimes called quantitative or continuous or interval or ratio level of measurement.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=29)** So things like time or distance or height or number of orders or any one of those things that you can measure quantitatively.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=39)** A few examples of this might include things like, for instance, the number of contacts you have in your customer relations management software, or the price of a product or a service that you offer, the distance of a commute you have to work or your employees as a whole, or things like improvement in well-being, which be measured on a 1 to 7 or a 0 to 10 scale, or the amount of time that a person is able to spend with family and with friends.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=66)** Any one of those is a quantitative variable where you're measuring more or less of something, and you can get a dot plot for it.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=73)** Now, when you're making a dot plot, what insights can you get?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=78)** Well, you're going to be looking for a few particular things in your graphic.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=81)** You're going to be looking for groups or clumps in the data.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=85)** Are there scores where there's an unusual high number of cases?
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=89)** And the flip side of that is, are there gaps or empty spaces in the data?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=93)** You're also going to be looking for symmetry.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=96)** Most procedures that you use in statistics like it when your data's symmetrical and most of it's in the middle and it tapers off to the left and the right, so that's going to be an important thing.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=106)** And then even more important is, do you have outliers?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=109)** And those are pretty easy to see in a dot plot.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=111)** Let me give you a fictional example, and then a few other examples.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=115)** So this is the same data about social media followers by platforms, and I've used in other examples.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=121)** And in this case, it's not labeled.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=122)** There are six platforms, but you you see we go from about 130 up to almost 600.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=128)** I can label the one directly.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=129)** I can say Facebook has 550 followers.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=132)** And if I'm trying to draw attention to that one in particular, this is an effective way of doing it.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=137)** You can also see that there's a great big gap there in the middle, and so we might want to find out why that's happening and what's going on there.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=144)** Truthfully, given the number of followers on different social media platforms, this makes sense.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=149)** Now I want to show you what certain kinds of distributions look like in dot plots.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=154)** This is a particular kind of dot plot for a uniform distribution, where the numbers from 0 to 10 and we have fractional values.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=162)** They're all essentially equally likely.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=165)** And so you can see that it's spread out in a uniform way.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=168)** Now this dot plot is jittered, and that means that the dots are randomly spread to the left or the right, so they're not piled high on top of each other.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=178)** They also have some transparencies, so it's darker when there's more than one in the same place.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=183)** But you can see it's basically all spread out.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=185)** You can also do what's called a stacked dot plot, where you don't put them on top of each other, but you just kind of put them all in this nice orderly arrangement left to right.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=195)** And you can see that it's basically the same thickness all the way through.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=200)** A normal distribution, which means a bell curve, looks like this.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=203)** Most of the dots are in the middle range, and then it tapers off to the higher values into the lower values.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=210)** If you looked at a stacked dot plot, it looks like this.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=215)** A positively skewed distribution is going to have most of the density, the dots and the darkness, at one end.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=222)** And you can see that it tapers out, and then things are kind of floating along.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=225)** We may have a couple of outliers there, and a stacked dot plot makes that pattern even clearer as we go through.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=232)** That's positively skewed, because the outliers, the unusual values, are at the positive or high end of the scores.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=238)** A negatively skewed distribution is simply the opposite of that.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=241)** Most of the scores are at the high end, and the outliers are on the low end.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=246)** And that's it, you can see the conglomeration of data points up there, and again, how it tapers out and you have only a small number there at the bottom.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=257)** And here's the same thing with the stacked dot plot.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=260)** In fact, it's cut off.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=261)** You don't see the full width at the top, but you do see how it tapers down as it comes.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=266)** A U-shaped distribution's pretty unusual, but it means most of your scores are at one extreme or the other.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=272)** And here you can see that the dots get really dense and the colors get dark, and that things are a little lighter in the middle.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=279)** This isn't an extreme example of a U-shaped distribution, but you can see how it curves in here on the stacked dot plot.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=286)** Any case, what all of these have in common is that they show you each individual observation in the data.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=294)** Sometimes people use dot plots when they only have a handful, like the social media example I showed you, there were only six dots.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=299)** Here I think there might be a thousand.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=302)** And you could start looking at density here, instead of trying to track an individual dot.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/dot-plots?u=76281980&t=307)** But either way, you're going to be able to see all of your data, and you're going to be able to start getting your own conclusions and your own generalizations as you do that visual analysis.

> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
