#### Box plots
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Box plots allow you to step back from your data a little bit.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=4)** They let you get more perspective on a quantitative or continuous variable.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=9)** So, instead of seeing the each individual point like you had with a dot plot, you get to break that dot plot down into just a few general components.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=19)** And what that lets you do when you have it in a box plot is it makes it very easy to judge the symmetry of the distribution, and to determine whether there are outliers in the distribution.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=29)** And that's what I use them for primarily.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=32)** Now, when you look for outliers there's a few reasons you might want to do that.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=36)** Those might be high-value cases.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=39)** So, you might have customers who are particularly profitable for you, or you might have services that are in unusually high demand.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=47)** You can also look for cases that need special attention.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=50)** If something's an outlier maybe it hasn't been addressed properly.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=55)** Also, there's quality control.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=57)** Maybe you have an error in your process, you forgot to turn on your social media profiles, your website was down, your ISP was down, or you have an unusual number of returns.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=68)** You can look for those kinds of outliers.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=70)** And then finally, by looking for outliers, or these unusual cases, you can look for new possibilities, things that you hadn't considered before, but because now it's right there and it's obviously separated from the rest you can give it the attention it deserves and see what kind of value you can get out of that.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=86)** Now, I want to show you how this works when we start with a dot plot.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=90)** This is a normal distribution by the way where most of the cases are in the middle, then it tapers off to the high and low values.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=97)** A box plot is simply something you can put on top of that, literally like this.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=101)** You can still see the dots underneath it.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=104)** And here's the anatomy of the box plot.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=107)** This box contains the middle 50% of the scores, so there's 50% that are on the outside.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=114)** The line right here in the middle is the median.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=117)** It splits the distribution into two equal halves, so half of the data are below that, half of the data are above that.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=124)** And when you have a skewed distribution this kind of measurement, the median, is especially important.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=130)** Down here we have 25% of the scores in what's called the first quartile.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=136)** They're quartiles 'cause we're splitting it into quarters, or four parts.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=140)** This part of the box has 25% of the scores in the second quartile.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=146)** This part of the box has 25% in the third, and then up here we have the last 25% of the scores in the fourth quartile.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=154)** So, we've split it up into four pieces, each of which has the same number of scores even though the scores have very different ranges in their values.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=163)** It's also worth pointing out this line here is actually called a whisker, and the full name for this is the box and whisker plot.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=171)** The whisker extends to the highest non-outlying score.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=175)** And in case you're wondering, the method by which something's determined an outlier has to do with the width of the box in the middle.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=183)** You actually make that 50% larger then tack it on to the end of the box, and anything beyond that's generally considered an outlier.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=189)** So, we have a few outliers on the end, but this line goes to the highest non-outlying score.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=193)** And then of course we have another whisker on the bottom that extends to the lowest non-outlying score.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=199)** And then outliers are marked separately.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=201)** We only have one here on the bottom.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=203)** It's marked twice because the dark dot in the middle is the box plot's point, and the gray one off to the side is the jittered point from the original dot plot.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=213)** And then we have a similar thing up here.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=214)** We've got three outlying scores that are marked separately.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=218)** The black ones correspond to the box plot, and the gray ones, which you see have the same value if you go horizontally, are from the jitter dot plot.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=228)** So, we have a progression, we start with the dots, we add the box to the dots, and then you can just get rid of the dots and have just the box, which makes it really easy to judge symmetry and the presence of outliers.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=242)** So, what insights can you get from a box plot?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=245)** Well, let's take a look at a few different distributions and see how it plays out.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=249)** Here is a uniform distribution where every number is equally likely.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=253)** The box it turns out in this case is really broad.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=257)** It's, well, it's half of the range here.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=260)** In this case it goes from 2.5 to 7.5.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=263)** That's what we expect from a distribution where every number is equally likely, it means that you're going to have the same range for each 25% of the distribution.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=272)** A normal distribution, where most of the scores are in the middle and things taper off, looks like this.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=277)** The box is much smaller, and you see we have just a small number of outliers.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=281)** When you have just a few outliers, and especially when you have 'em on both sides, that lets you know that it's not usually a problem.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=289)** That's just what happens with a normal distribution.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=292)** So, we have these three outliers up here.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=294)** It's good to know they're there.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=295)** There are not too many of them, they're not too far out, not a major problem.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=298)** With a positively skewed distribution you can see we have a lot of outliers.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=304)** That's the most important thing to learn from this one.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=306)** Outliers will throw off nearly any analysis you will do.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=309)** And so, number one, being apprised of their existence and knowing where they are and how far are they out, which is what we see really easily with a box plot, is important.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=318)** And then the negative box plot obviously is the same thing on the other direction.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=322)** We have a significant number of outliers, and that's something that could drastically affect our results if we don't take care of it properly.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=331)** A U-shaped distribution, where people tend to clump at one end or the other, you can see that the range here for the box in the middle is even bigger because most of those scores fall into the whisker area at the top or the bottom.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=345)** And this giant box here is your clue that something here is a little unusual.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=350)** I can show you another variation on the giant box with what's called a combined distribution.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=355)** So, we've got this really big box.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=357)** And in fact, the bottom part of it's enormous.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=360)** Well, when you have something like this sometimes it happens because you have multiple distributions mixed together.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=368)** In this particular data set, which comes from the measurement of iris flowers, it turns out that there are three different species.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=375)** And if you break it down and do box plots for them independently, and that's one of the great things you can do with box plots, then you can see maybe we don't want to be averaging these three different things because there's so much separation between them.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=387)** And that's the value of the box plot.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=389)** It's a clue that something's going on.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=391)** You can break it down, and then you can check individual variability, break it down by different groups, or maybe even have box plots for separate variables right next to each other.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/box-plots?u=76281980&t=400)** But a box plot is going to be really great for judging symmetry and for judging outliers, and that's going to make a big influence on how you do your analyses.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), this. (2), extends (2), finally, (1), throw (1)

#### Histograms
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've looked at box plots, which are wonderful for judging the symmetry of a distribution and the presence of outliers.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=7)** But histograms do a different thing.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=9)** They let you know about differences in shape, the shape of your data.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=14)** Specifically, they answer a vague question, what does your data look like?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=18)** Is it symmetrical, something we can do with box plots, but you get a different impression of it with histograms.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=24)** Is there more than one peak, something that is much clearer with a histogram?
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=29)** And are there gaps or other unusual values?

> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/histograms?u=76281980&t=32)** And so this is a compliment to box plots.

> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sparklines?u=76281980&t=263)** And so, for instance, if you had different kinds of information, say for instance, positive information, bad information, weird information, you could use different emojis to represent it in the graphs.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sparklines?u=76281980&t=273)** And that way, you're giving both some sort of interpretation at the same time that you're giving the representation of the data.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sparklines?u=76281980&t=282)** Anyhow, sparklines are wonderful, both the official sparklines, and the little hacked repeat chart of ways of including the visualization right there in the cells next to the data, to get the data close at hand, and make it as easy as possible, as seamless as possible to go from data, to visualization, to action.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), this. (1), finally, (1), case, (1)

> **Analogies:** for instance (3)
> **Env Vars:** rept (2)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (1), click on (1)

>

>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=96)** The fact is they hear as everywhere else.
>

>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=109)** So be cautious as you do this.

>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=121)** Like every other analysis in the world, it is inherently and inescapably correlational.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=129)** We may want to make cause and effect conclusions.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=132)** And while we can probably get close enough for any practical purposes, it's theoretically impossible to have anything other than correlational data.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=140)** So again, be circumspect.

>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=151)** Now, that's often not the case.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=154)** And so you may have to do some sort of transformation to the data, or you may have to do a different kind of modeling, but the assumption is linear until you have evidence otherwise.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=165)** And then finally there's the issue of extrapolation.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=167)** And what that means is making predictions for cases that fall outside the range of the observed data that you use to make your model.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=174)** This is always a very, very dangerous practice 'cause it's going to lead you astray because while things may be linear or they may fall within a certain pattern or within a range, going even more than a month or a few months into the future, things can change.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=188)** And so you need to be very careful about extrapolation.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=192)** Instead you want to focus on interpolation, that's within the ranges of the observed data.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=198)** But with those things is in mind, what kind of insights can you get from scatterplots and from regression?

>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=402)** So for instance, this one here at the top left, this is the relationship between sepal length and sepal width in irish flowers.

>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=411)** There's pretty much no relationship.
>

>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=422)** And the numbers that are on the top right of the diagonal are correlation coefficients which I will talk about in another video in this course.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=430)** But in this matrix, you have several different plots.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=434)** So for instance, there's the sepal length and there's the sepal width, but we can also come down to this one and see sepal width versus petal length.
>
> **[7:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=441)** And now we see a very strong downward association, but there's also this really troubling separation within the data, which might not work well normally.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=452)** And you'd want to look at that a little more closely.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=454)** And then here, for instance, this one is petal length versus petal width and it's a very strong positive relationship.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=460)** And everything's coming really close to the line, which is great, even though we have this big separation near the bottom.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=466)** But what this does is it shows you the relationship between two quantitative or continuous variables at a time.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=474)** And it puts several of them together to give you the bigger picture on what's happening with your data, and hopefully even answer some of the questions that might rise when you're looking at one particular plot that you can get this insight by sort of triangulating between them.
>
> **[8:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/scatterplots?u=76281980&t=488)** And so the scatterplot along with the regression line in here is a really great way of looking at these kinds of relationships to ask important questions and define the potential for valuable insight in your data.

#### Data maps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's nice to know where you are and what's around you, and having a map can help you find your place in real space, but a similar thing can happen with the data.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=11)** Finding the patterns across geographical patterns can also lead you to great insights.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=18)** One of the best ways to do this with data, in a very simple way, is what's called a choropleth map now.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=25)** Choropleth comes from two Greek words, Choros which means area or region, and plethos, which means multitudes.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=34)** So it means showing multitudes by areas.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=36)** It's showing a quantitative value.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=39)** How much of something by a region.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=42)** And so for instance, here's one that I made in Google Sheets.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=46)** And personally, I find these maps are a little easier to make in Google Sheets than they are in Excel, but there are a lot of other options as well.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=53)** But this is a choropleth map of searches across the U.S. in the year 2020 for the term, Yoga Studio.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=61)** And what you see here is that the lighter colors indicate fewer searches relatively speaking, and the darker colors indicate more.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=71)** So our range goes from 13 to 100.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=75)** And you can see for instance, that Vermont, there in the upper right corner, led the country in relative popularity of searches for yoga studios in 2020, so they get the darkest.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=85)** But it's nice to see the pattern.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=87)** And you can see that things are kind of bunching into the upper right part of the U.S. map.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=93)** And so this is a good way to get started.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=96)** And by the way, if you do this in Google Sheets it also is live and you can hover over each of these and get the values, as opposed to the screenshot that I have here.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=106)** Just another way to show this, I got some data that was showing, not the percentage of something, but instead was showing the number of historically Black colleges and universities in the United States broken down by state.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=121)** And what you see from this is, they are almost exclusively in the Southern United States or the South and Eastern part of the United States with one exception, there in California.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=133)** It goes from as few as well, the gray ones show that there are no HBCUs in that particular state, but it goes up to as many as 12.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=142)** And this is a nice way of seeing a very strong pattern.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=146)** It makes it really obvious in a way that you would not get by looking at the table.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=150)** Another option when doing this in Google Sheets, is instead of coloring the entire state, is to just show the pins.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=157)** And these are pins for the city that the historically Black colleges and universities are in.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=164)** And we have from one up to as many as six in the same city or metropolitan area.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=170)** And again, it shows the very strong pattern in the South and the Southeast, primarily.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=176)** Making a map, getting this kind of picture, is a very quick and very easy way of getting some of these really obvious insights, like the extreme concentration of schools in the South, by looking at the popularity of things across the entire country.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/data-maps?u=76281980&t=191)** By looking at how many people are searching for your website, how many people are buying your products, how many people you're engaging with, in a state-by-state or a country-by-country basis by simply making a map, a choropleth, that shows data values by regions, can be a great way to get started on that.

### 5. Describe Data

#### Numerical descriptions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A lot of practical decisions can be made by applying what's called the interocular trauma test to your data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=7)** Make a graph and see if anything hits you between the eyes.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=11)** However, when you're working with larger data sets and when more's at stake, like a person's health or wellbeing, or maybe the margins in an extremely competitive market, then you'll only need more nuance and precision in your analysis.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=24)** And that's where numerical descriptions come in.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=27)** We're going to look at some of the most common and most useful ways to describe data numerically.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=32)** These include ways of describing the center of a collection or distribution of numbers, some of the measures of variability that tell you how spread out things are.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=41)** We'll look at some methods to rescale scores in a distribution to make them easier to understand and to work with, and then finally, we'll look at a few ways of assessing the associations between variables with measures of effect size, with correlations, and regression.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=57)** Now, one important qualification is that we're going to look at all of these procedures as descriptive approaches, not as the more complicated influential procedures, but really as a way of simply describing the data and trying to see what's in front of you.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=72)** That's something that makes it easy to do in spreadsheets as well as a lot of other packages.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/numerical-descriptions?u=76281980&t=76)** But let's take a look at how each of these can work in practice and how you can get a little more meaning out of them.

>

>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=261)** And one of the neat things about the mean is it is literally the balance point of a distribution.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=266)** If you were to carve this out of wood and put it on a seesaw, the mean is where it would balance.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=272)** Now, the mean is also very statistical.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=275)** It has a formula.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=276)** And if you're familiar with Greek, this is the formula for the population mean, where that's a mu on the side.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=281)** And it says to sum up the scores on X and divide by the number of scores.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=286)** There's a more familiar one for the sample, which is X bar, where the bar over the top means get the mean of that.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=293)** Or the American Psychological Association.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=296)** A lot of people write an APA style.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=298)** They recommend this generic version, just a big capital M for mean.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=301)** You add up the scores, and you divide by the number of scores.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=304)** And of course it's really easy to do this in a spreadsheet.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=306)** Just use the average function.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=309)** But I do want to show you very quickly, if we take the values of all the boxes I showed you a minute ago, a one and a one and a one and so on, and we add those all up and divide by 12 'cause that's how many boxes there were, then what we get is a sum of 48 divided by 12.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=324)** Well, that's four, and so that's our mean.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=326)** And that's why that balance point is exactly right there at four.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=331)** And so the mean is a very familiar calculation.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=335)** And it works really well for quantitative or continuous data where you have set distances where you know how far apart all of the scores are.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=342)** And it's very useful in analysis because it goes into things like the standard deviation, the correlation, regression.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=349)** So many other procedures rely on the mean.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=352)** However, it's very fragile.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=354)** There are things that can happen to it.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=355)** I just want to finish by showing you some of that.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=358)** So imagine we've got this nice, symmetrical data set.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=362)** We've got these numbers one through seven.

>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=367)** It's really easy.

>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=386)** And if we add an open-ended score, that's like six plus or an undefined, DNF means did not finish in arrays.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=392)** Well, the spreadsheet is still able to give us numbers, but what you need to know is those two numbers in red, they simply ignored those last two values.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=400)** Under normal circumstances, it's simply not possible to calculate the mean when you have these kinds of things going on.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=407)** And so that lets you know that the mean is very useful in a lot of situations, but it does have some of its limitations, which is when you want to use the median.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=414)** But when you're looking for value, the first thing you want to do is is your distribution symmetrical?
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=420)** And do you have outliers or open-ended scores?

>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-measures-of-center?u=76281980&t=437)** But any of those, the mean, the median, and the mode can serve as an excellent first point in describing an entire set of data with just a single number, and getting to the point as quickly as possible.

#### Describe variability with the variance and standard deviation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The range and the IQR are wonderful measures for figuring out the boundaries of what you're dealing with, and finding the particularly valuable central area of your data set.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=11)** But really the most common measures of variability in statistics are the variance and the standard deviation.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=18)** Now, these are so closely related, they're often treated as one, and actually it's not just these two, there are different versions of these for whether you're dealing with a sample or a population of scores.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=30)** It's not going to make a difference for you 'cause you're going to always be dealing with the sample version, but I want to show you how these things work, and the important thing is to remember that these are basically an average distance of the each score from the mean, and I have to put average in quotes because there's some variations on it and you'll see how these work in practice.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=51)** But the first thing we need to talk about is something called the sum of squares, which is actually short for the sum of squared deviations from the mean.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=59)** Now in the real world, you don't need to work with this because the computer will do it for you, spreadsheets, you just tell them you want the variance, you just tell them you want the standard deviation, and it's over and done with it.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=69)** But I do want to demonstrate it just so you understand a little bit about what goes into it, even if you're not going to be calculating it by hand.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=78)** So here's our data set right here, and what we're going to calculate is this sum of squares.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=83)** It has a formula here, and what this means is that you're going to get the sum, that's the big Greek Sigma there, of each score X minus the mean, so how far is each score from the mean, how much does it deviate from the mean.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=98)** You're going to take that deviation, you're going to square it for each case and then add it all up, the that's the sum of squares.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=104)** Now that's the formula but I want to show you there's a visual explanation to it because truthfully every single thing we do in data can be done visually.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=113)** So let me show you how that works, the first thing we do is we mark where the mean is and it's right here at four, we know that from before, and let's take all of our boxes to make them a little lighter, so we can focus on the calculations between them.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=128)** Let's put a dot right here at the mean, so there's the mean.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=131)** Now, let's go down to the score on the left, the three and put a dot there, and we can see that they are one step apart from each other.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=139)** Well, we can now make a square that has that distance on each side and there's this square, and then we can get the area of the square.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=148)** So that is the squared deviation from the mean for that particular score, the three.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=153)** And we can make this little clearer and do it for each individual box.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=157)** So for instance, we've got another one unit square here, another one there, and then we go down to the left, it's two squares down, so it's got a bigger square, then another one, one over here, then the seven is going to be three squares or three units on each side, and then the three ones each of them is going to have a square that size, and then the 11 on the far side is going to have a enormous square.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=180)** But if you take the area of each of these squares and add it up, that is the sum of squares, it is the sum of the square deviations from the mean.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=191)** And that's what this formula gives us, and so there is a visual explanation to it.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=195)** Now in this particular data set, it so happens that this sum of squares is equal to exactly 100, that's very convenient, but it's coincidental, and then if we want to calculate the variance of the standard deviation, we use that sum of squares in four different versions of formulas.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=211)** The variance has a formula for the population.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=216)** Now, in most situations you don't need to worry about populations versus samples.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=219)** A population is every possible case that you could be concerned about, and a sample is the actual people or cases that you have data from, and so here we have Sigma squared, that's a lowercase Greek Sigma, you just take the sum of the squares and need to divide it by N, or if you have a sample, you divide it by n-1, that's actually called the degrees of freedom.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=242)** And so you get slightly different answers depending on whether it's a population or a sample, and that's because samples tend to under-represent the variability of the populations that they came from for some obscure mathematical reasons, but you do have to make this adjustment, and if you're using this spreadsheet, it will ask you whether you want this population or the sample version.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=262)** The sample versions are the default is the one you should always use 'cause you actually have the sample data in front of you.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=267)** The standard deviation is just one more step on each of those, all you do is you take the square root, and the reason for that is because the variant has to do with these squared deviations, but you really want to be back in your original units, and so taking the square root does that.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=281)** So let me show you how this works with what we had a moment ago.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=284)** Here's the formula for the sample standard deviation which can write as either a lowercase S or the APA in other organizations, want you to just write a big capital SD for standard deviation.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=295)** So we've got this formula, we're going to stick in our values that the sum squares of 100 the sample size is 12, 12 minus one is equal to 11, 100 divided by 11 is equal to 9.09, take the square root of that, and you get 3.02.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=311)** That's our standard deviation, but now let me show you how that relates to the data that we had earlier.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=317)** So here's our data set again and there's the mean right there at four the standard deviation goes up three points from 4 to 7.03, and it goes down three points to just below one, that tells you the average distance, again, average here in quotes, the average distance of each point from the mean.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=339)** Now, you can see that the 11 is really far away it's an outlier and as such it exerts undue influence.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=346)** But the average here is important, the variance and the standard deviation, while they're not familiar terms to most people, they are used in a lot of statistical procedures, so they're very important, and again, it's essentially an average of the distance from each score to the mean, and like the mean it also something that's very fragile, I just want to show you here.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=367)** Now, in our regular data set here we have a standard deviation of 1.76, but if we change this 7 on the bottom to a 34, you see that it go us all the way up to 8.93 and the variance increases much, much more because it's on squared units.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=382)** And the open ended and undefined scores, Well, these are in red because you can tell what it's done is simply ignored these values completely, and that's normally not what you want to do, but it's one way of addressing the fragility of the procedure.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-variability-with-the-variance-and-standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=397)** And so the point here is you want to know how spread out your data is, and these measures the range, the interquartile range, or the variance and the standard deviation, are all different measures that give you an idea of how that works, and they're going to be really useful for comparing them directly from one data set to another, or from one month to another, or from one quarter to another, and that's going to give you a feel for the variability and really the distinctiveness of the data that you have from one time to another.

> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=112)** 95% will be from five 5' to 5'8.

> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=115)** And 99.7% will be between 4'10 and 5'10.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=120)** Now where I am in Utah, we've got a lot of Scandinavian people.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=123)** So got to move all that up about two inches.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=126)** But this lets you know if you're buying apparel for your shop and you want to be able to accommodate 95% of the people who walk in the door, then you probably need to be prepared for clothing that fits people from 5' tall to 5'8.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=143)** And that's where you get the Z-scores and the proportions as a practical decision making matter.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=150)** And if you want to do Z-scores for height, you can take those Heights and you can also switch them into the individual Z-scores.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=155)** So let's say you have a customer who's 5'7.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=159)** They are one and a half standard deviations above the mean 'cause remember the mean's 5'4 and standard deviation is two.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=167)** So one and a half times two gets you to 5'7.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=170)** If they're 5'2, then they're two inches below the mean of 5'4, which is one standard deviation.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=176)** So they have a Z-score of negative one.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=179)** And you can use this to convert people from different heights or say for instance you're looking at something like weight because here in America we use pounds.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=187)** Over in Europe, they use kilograms.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=189)** And if you're in the UK, you might use stone.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=193)** My scale has a setting for that.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=194)** And I can't convert the numbers in my head.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=197)** But if you gave me Z-scores, I would always know whether a person was above or below the mean and by some idea of how much.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=205)** So when you get a Z-score which tells you how many standard deviations above or below the mean a particular score is, couple of very simple things to keep in mind.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=214)** Number one, if the Z-score is positive, that means the person score is above the mean.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=219)** If their score is negative, that means they are below the mean.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=224)** And if their score is zero, it means they're exactly on the mean.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=228)** Their score is equal to the mean.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=230)** And so that is the first part of it.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=233)** And then you can use the absolute value to determine how far from the mean and you can use it to compare different distributions.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=240)** But you do need to pay attention to a couple of things as you do all of this.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=244)** First extreme Z-scores, something with that's more than three, if you get a Z-score of like nine or negative six, that's going to set off alarms.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=253)** It could mean that the distribution isn't a bell curve.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=256)** If it's strongly skewed and income and house prices are strongly skewed, then you might get a Z-score like that.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=262)** You might get, say for instance, here in Utah, if somebody has a house that costs $8 million, they might have a Z-score of nine or 12 or something because they are so far out on the distribution.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=273)** It also could mean that the scores from a different distribution, say for instance you're comparing movie revenues and you have book revenues.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=283)** They're different scales.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=284)** They shouldn't be in the same thing.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=286)** Or if you're comparing the height of adults to kindergartners, you're going to get unusual Z-scores.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=291)** It's a clue that something's in there that shouldn't be.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=293)** But what all of this does is that lets you take scores from an arbitrary or unfamiliar metric and convert it to this common metric that especially when you're dealing with a bell curve is very easy to interpret and it's always no matter what easy to compare from one distribution to another.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/interpret-z-scores?u=76281980&t=311)** And that makes it easier for you to identify exceptional cases and possibly some exceptional opportunities in your work.

#### Describe group differences with effect sizes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980)

>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=146)** that's similar to Z-scores, that allows you to say how many standard deviations above or below a group mean something is, again, because that turns everything into the same kind of metric, and when we're comparing groups, we want to change the metric or whatever unit of measurement you're using to units of standard deviations because that you to compare more easily from one on one group to another.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=169)** Also, the nice thing about the standardization of these effect sizes is that it includes the variability of the scores in both groups.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=177)** So it's not just the mean or the percent, but it also looks at how spread out things are within the groups, and so you may recall that I talked about the Z-score, which is a way of looking at how far above or below the group mean a individual score or a smaller group score is, and we did that by taking in the score, X, subtracting from the mean to find out the difference and then dividing by the standard deviation, which tells us how many standard deviations they are away from the mean.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=208)** Well, there is a similar method that is used for effect sizes, and it's called Cohen's d.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=216)** Here, if we're going to be comparing two groups, we take the mean of the first one, which I'm just writing as M, subscript one, and then we subtract the mean of the second one, M, subscript two, and then we divide that by the standard deviation.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=229)** Now, the P subscript there stands for pooled, and if your groups are very different sizes, then there's some slightly complicated mathematics, but if your groups are about, basically, the same, you can just average or split the difference between the two standard deviations, and let me give you an example of how that works with the spending data.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=248)** So here we have again, the Midwest and the South, and we have the mean or the average amount of spending per household per year.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=256)** So 3,292 in the Midwest, 2,639 in the South.
>

>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=264)** The tables that I got from the Bureau of Labor Statistics didn't give the standard deviations but based on what I know about standard deviations and total household income, I estimated the standard deviations to be around 1,300 for the Midwest and about 1,000 for the South.
>

>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=285)** So we need the mean for the first group and the mean for the second group, get the difference between them.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=289)** That's what subtraction does, and then divide by the standard deviation of the two groups.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=296)** Now, I'll do this by taking the average for the Midwest, $3,292, subtract the average for the South, $2,639, divide by the sum of the standard deviations, which I estimated at 1,300 and $1,000 and divided by two to get the average of those two.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=315)** So that gives us a difference of $653, divided by a roughly pooled standard deviation of $1,150.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=325)** Do that division, and you get 0.57, and what that tells us is that the average entertainment spending in the Midwest was a little more than half a standard deviation above the average entertainment spending in the South.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=342)** That is a common metric.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=344)** It's the lingua franca, the shared language, that we can use to make comparisons.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=349)** The next question is, is that a big number or not?
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=352)** It's a little over half a standard deviation.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=354)** Well, this is where we get to go to a commonly-used reference.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=359)** This formula is called Cohen's d because it was developed by a psychological researcher, Jacob Cohen, and he also published some rough guidelines for what constitutes a small, medium or big difference.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=373)** He said, "small is around 0.2."
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=377)** That means two tenths of a standard deviation.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=380)** Medium would be about .5, and ours was slightly larger than that.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=385)** So ours, by these very rough rules of thumb, would be considered a medium effect size, and the large one would be .8, or eight tenths of a standard deviation, or larger, and so those are some general ideas of saying how big is big, and the idea is that the difference in annual entertainment spending between the Midwest and the South appears to be just larger than medium, and so it's something that is worth paying attention to.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=414)** Another place where you can apply these general concepts is anytime you're comparing the means of two groups.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=420)** So for instance, if you're doing A-B testing on a website design, which compares the mean response to one kind of design to the mean response of a second, you can do the same thing.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=430)** The thing to note is that the sign that means positive or negative, plus or minus, is arbitrary.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=435)** It just depends on which group you put first, but the values are unbounded.
>
> **[7:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-group-differences-with-effect-sizes?u=76281980&t=439)** They can go from zero, which is when the groups are absolutely identical, but it can go to positive or negative infinity, depending on how large the differences are, but the nice thing about it is you can always use it to compare one intervention, say, for instance, one website design, to another, and you can compare it across different context, across different times, and all of that, it serves the purpose of helping you get interpretable insights from your data that are sensitive to context, so you know how to make the best possible decisions based on your data.

#### Predict scores with regression
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you're working in the business world and the ability to predict what's going to happen is an amazing thing.
>

>
