>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=316)** Are the points close to the regression line?
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=319)** Regression is more useful when the data cluster by the line.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=322)** Otherwise what's the point of having the line if everything is scattered all over the place?
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=327)** Third, are there outliers?
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=328)** Are there extreme values on either one access or the other or unusual combinations?
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=335)** And it turns out that in regression unusual combinations are particularly influential.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=340)** So you want to check for those.

>

>

>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=360)** Now, there's one other thing I want to remind you about.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=363)** And that's interpolation versus extrapolation.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=367)** Interpolation means you're staying within the observed range of both X and Y.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=372)** So you're staying within the bounds of your current data.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=376)** You are inside the poles and that's going to get you predictions that are more accurate and more generalizable, and that's what you want.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=385)** So stay within the bounds of the current data.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=387)** And that's very safe usually.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=390)** That's compared to extrapolation which means you're going extra or outside the poles of your data, the far ends.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=397)** Going outside of range on X, so if you have data that says people from three minutes to 90 minutes, and you say, well what if somebody's been on there for 500 minutes?
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=406)** That can lead to really serious errors.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=409)** You can get way off by assuming that the linear growth is consistent all the way across.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=416)** It might be but it may also taper off after a certain point.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=420)** That's often true with things like investments and expenditures.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=423)** So you need to be very careful about going outside of your observed data.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=428)** But if you can keep these things in mind, is the pattern linear?
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=431)** The points close to it, are there no outliers?
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=434)** And are we trying to make predictions that fit within the bounds of the existing data?
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/predict-scores-with-regression?u=76281980&t=438)** Then regression can be a very powerful way of summarizing a simple relationship and making some quick predictions that can help you get value out of your own data.

#### Describe associations with correlations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980)

> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=65)** The other ones have different patterns.

> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=67)** So, even though you can calculate the numbers for them, they're not going to be as informative as they would be for the top left one, where the data follows the nice, straight line pattern.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=77)** Now, let's take a look at different scatterplots and the strength of correlations, the most common kind of correlation is what's called the Pearson product moment correlation coefficient, which is usually just abbreviated as r.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=91)** And so what I have here are nine scatterplots that show different strength of association as measured by r.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=98)** Let's take a look here at the top left.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=100)** This is when an r is equal to positive 1.0, and what that means it is a perfect linear association.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=107)** If you know somebody's score on x, you can predict their score on y perfectly.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=111)** And it's an uphill one going from the bottom left to the top right, and every dot is exactly on the line.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=117)** That basically never happens except for when you're, say, converting from miles to kilometers.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=122)** Right next to it is a correlation of positive 0.75, and now you can see that the points are spreading out a little bit.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=130)** That's still considered a very strong relationship.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=133)** Normally the upper bounds are going to be something more like this positive .50, and you can see things are spread out a little more and the lines, a little flatter, but there's still noticeable pattern.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=146)** Here, we have a positive 0.25, and you start doing real data, you're going to get a lot of data like this.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=153)** The line is slightly sloped and things are kind of spread out all over, but there is a detectable pattern, and this is why you want to do the correlation analysis because truthfully, this 0.25 is hard to tell sometimes from this 0.00, where there is no linear association at all.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=172)** You see that the line there in the middle is totally flat, and the data's just a blob.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=176)** Knowing x doesn't tell you anything about y, and then we rotate to the negative.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=181)** This one is slightly negative.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=182)** Negative 0.25.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=185)** And then a little stronger at negative 0.50.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=188)** Then we got a strong one.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=189)** You see how the line is tilting down even more.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=193)** And the data points are closer and closer to the line.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=196)** Until finally, we have a negative 1.0.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=198)** It is a perfect linear association that's negative going downhill.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=203)** And so, these are variations on correlation coefficients, and they can tell you something about the strength of the association between the two variables.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=211)** Now, there is a formula for this.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=213)** You're not going to do it by hand because you're going to use software, a spreadsheet or some other application to tell you.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=218)** But I do want to mention that what you're dealing with here is actually the average cross product of standardized scores.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=225)** So, if you have two variables and you convert everybody's score in each variable to a z-score, that tells you how many standard deviations above or below the mean they are, you get z-scores on both of those for everybody, you multiply those two z-scores that's called a cross product, and then you average those cross products that gives you the correlation coefficient.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=244)** And a few things to know about the correlation coefficient.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=247)** Number one is the range.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=249)** It goes from negative one to positive one.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=252)** That's what we saw in that matrix of the nine scatterplots.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=257)** Also that the sign of the association, the sign, the plus or minus part, simply tells you the direction.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=263)** Is it uphill?
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=264)** That's positive Or, is it downhill?
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=266)** That's negative.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=267)** And then separate from that is the strength.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=270)** That's the absolute value, the number part, and that tells you how strong the association is between those two variables.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/describe-associations-with-correlations?u=76281980&t=277)** As long as you're talking about a linear association, and really, that suggests that we need to have a measure of effect size for correlation and for regression, which is what I want to talk about in the next video.

#### Effect size for correlation and regression
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The ability to describe associations with correlations and predict scores with regression is something that can be really, really helpful in trying to decide what's going to be the best predictors, what you can use to best understand what's happening with for instance, the people you're working with.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=18)** And it's good to be able to compare them.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=20)** So for instance, to be able to say that one correlation is larger than another so you know that it's a better predictor.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=26)** There is one major catch on this, however and that's that correlations are not linear values.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=34)** They don't increase in a linear fashion.

>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=41)** It's definitely stronger, but if you want to compare them, you need to take this extra step.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=47)** And what that is is going from correlations to something called R squared and the PVE.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=54)** Now, you square the correlation coefficient, R to get R squared and it goes to a capital R because that's referring to a situation where you can actually have several predictor variables, but when you do R squared, it's written in capitals.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=68)** And what R squared which is also called the coefficient of determination tells you is the proportion of variance in Y that can be explained or predicted by X, okay?
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=82)** So if you know X, how much of the variance in Y can you explain or predict?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=87)** And that's known as the PVE or the proportion of variance explained.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=93)** There are several measures of effect size that fall into that category.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=97)** R squared is one of them, but let me give you an example of how this works.

>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=195)** If you square that, you get to 0.1 and in terms of the proportion of variance explained, you just convert that proportion to a percentage and that's 1%.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=203)** It's not much, but it is something that shows up in the real world.

>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=215)** And then large might be a correlation of 0.5 where you square that and you get an R squared of 0.25 and the proportion of variance explained is 25%.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=225)** Now that's going to depend a lot on the field that you're working in.
>

>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=240)** But the single most important thing to remember when interpreting correlations and associations is that the meaningfulness depends on your context.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/effect-size-for-correlation-and-regression?u=76281980&t=248)** That is, you know your business and you know what makes sense and what's useful.

> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/exploring-tables?u=76281980&t=71)** Here, I tell it that I want session and I want the columns to show the day.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/exploring-tables?u=76281980&t=76)** It's an easy way to organize the data and to get the summary values.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/exploring-tables?u=76281980&t=79)** And again, whichever spreadsheet program you're using, I encourage to get a course that talks a little bit more about the creation of the pivot tables.

### 6. Probability and Inference

#### Basic probability
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Life is uncertain.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=2)** And it's not just a matter of whether you should take an umbrella with you when you leave home.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=8)** Lots of things have some ambiguity and indeterminacy around them.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=15)** That can include things like trying to come up with sales projections for the next quarter or trying to figure out whether your intended routine is going to have the desired health effects, or even if you're on a movie studio, trying to figure out whether a movie is likely to be a hit or a flop.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=32)** All of these are hard to do, but basic probability gives us a method for getting to these.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=40)** It has to do with the same kind of logic that goes behind flipping a coin and determining the likelihood of a heads or a tails.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=50)** Now, I'm going to give you demonstrations of several different principles across several videos.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=57)** This one looks at basic probability, or simple probability.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=60)** Another one looks at conditional probability where one thing depends on another.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=66)** Then we have expected values, and then sampling variation, and we'll conclude with a very brief introduction to statistical inference which you can think of as trying to describe a population that is only partially observed.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=81)** And that's an important part of applied data analysis.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=84)** But let's go to the basic elements of probability.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=89)** A sample space means all the things that you are drawing from when you're trying to infer.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=94)** And what I have here are 16 points.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=97)** And they're shown as squares and circles, some are blue, some are orange.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=103)** And the idea here is this is the list of options that we're dealing with, the same way like when you flip a coin, you can have heads or tails, or when you flip a single dye you can have a one, two, three, four, five, or six.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=115)** Those are your sample spaces.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=117)** This one we have 16 possible outcomes, although several of them resemble each other.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=121)** I want to show you the elements of basic probabilities.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=125)** So if we have these 16 things, imagine them in a hat and you mix them all up and you start pulling things out.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=132)** What is the probability of drawing a thing that is orange?
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=137)** Well, that's pretty simple.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=138)** We're looking at these four things across the top.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=140)** And if we actually want to put numbers on it, what we do is we say the probability of something that is orange.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=147)** And so we put probability and then in parentheses, the thing that we're trying to get the probability of, is equal to the number of things, n, that are orange divided by the number of all things, the things in our sample space.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=161)** And in that case, this is 16.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=162)** So the probability of orange is four because that's four orange things divided by 16 things total.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=170)** And when you do four divided by 16, you get a proportion which is 0.25, that's the probability.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=176)** Although more frequently people like to see it as a percentage.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=180)** So you can say the probability of randomly drawing an orange object out of this sample space of 16 objects is 25%.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=188)** So that's easy.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=189)** Four orange objects out of 16.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=191)** You have a 25% of randomly drawing an orange item.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=196)** Now, if you want to write this as a formula, which I don't expect you to do, but if you want to be a little more formal about it, this is an option.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=203)** You're looking at the probability of event A in sample space S, those are the common terms used.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=211)** And so we write probability, and then in parentheses of A for event or object A is equal to nA, that's the number of things that count as A divided by the number of things in the sample space, the total group that we're dealing with.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=227)** If you want to be a little more concise, probability can usually just be abbreviated as p.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=233)** So p or probability of A is equal to nA divided by nS.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=239)** But let's do it with pictures again, 'cause that's a little simpler.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=243)** Let's look at the probability of getting a circle.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=246)** So we have these 16 objects and a hat and a bowl, and we're going to pull them out.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=250)** What's the probability that we got a circle?
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=252)** Well, that could be these three orange circles, or it could be these three blue circles.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=259)** And so what we're looking at is the probability of circle is the number of circles divided by the number of objects in the sample space.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=266)** There are six circles out of 16 total objects, which gives us a proportion or probability of 0.375.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=273)** And again, if you want that as a percentage, you can write it as 37.5%.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=277)** So we have a 37.5% chance of randomly drawing a circle from these 16 objects.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=284)** That's nice, it's pretty abstract.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=286)** And maybe you wonder where this shows up in real life.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=290)** Well, think about it.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=292)** When a person walks into your brick and mortar store or they find their way to your online store, what's the probability that they'll make a purchase?
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=302)** You have to look at the total number of people who have come into your store or come to your website, that's your sample space.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=308)** And then you're going to compare that to the number of people who have actually made purchases.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=313)** And that's how you get the probability of a purchase when a person walks in or visits your store.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=319)** Another one is what's the probability of a business thriving after say, five years?
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=325)** Starting businesses, that's a lot of hard work and it's uncertain.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=328)** And what you have to do is get historical data.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=331)** How many people attempted to start a business?
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=334)** And that's going to be your sample space, the number of businesses that start on day one.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=338)** And then five years later, how many of them are still active and profitable?
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=345)** That's the A that you're looking for.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=347)** So profitable businesses divided by all businesses started five years ago.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=352)** Or if you're into bike racing, there's something called the tour divide.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=356)** That's a 2,700 mile mountain bike race down the Great Divide mountains from Banff, Canada through the US to the border with Mexico.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=364)** Not surprisingly not everybody who starts this extremely long race which takes weeks to get through finishes.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=371)** But if you want the sample space, that's everybody who begins the race.
>

>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=382)** And that will give you the simple or basic probability.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=385)** And so those things are all pretty simple.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=389)** There are a few things, however, that complicate this, and that is sometimes you don't have complete information about the population.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=396)** And you just only know that people who have come into your store, you're not able to compare them against the whole population, or you've only been keeping track of some of the data and not the rest.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=408)** It makes it impossible to estimate with any accuracy of probability, if you don't even know what your denominator is, if you don't know what the scores are in your sample space.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=418)** Even worse is when you don't even know how large the sample space is.
>
> **[7:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=422)** It may be that you know three people who've won the lottery.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=425)** Well, that's pretty spectacular, but how many people have bought lottery tickets?
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=430)** You have to know that number, and it's going to be millions or hundreds of millions in order to accurately calculate the probability.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=437)** But if you can get the outcomes measured for the relevant cases that you're concerned about, and if you can get the data about how large it is, then you're going to have the most basic elements to find a probability.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/basic-probability?u=76281980&t=453)** And that's going to give you a compass or a guide to help you make some of the applied decisions that you confront in your own business or your personal life.

#### Conditional probability

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When it comes to probability, you're not always starting from scratch.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=4)** Sometimes, you already have something, a piece of information that gets you part way there.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=9)** And conditional probabilities are a good example of that boost, that first step that has already happened.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=18)** Now, I just showed you an example of how simple or basic probabilities work.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=22)** That's the probability of something all on its own.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=24)** So for instance, we looked at the probability of drawing one of these 16 objects at random, and getting something that's orange, and there's four out of 16.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=34)** So the probability is 25%.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=36)** Or the probability of getting a circle out of these 16.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=40)** And there are six circles, so we have six out of 16.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=44)** Those are basic or simple probabilities 'cause it's just that one thing on its own in the sample space.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=51)** On the other hand, conditional probabilities answer a slightly different question.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=55)** They start with an assumption, if B is true, if one thing we know is already true, then what is the probability of this other thing, A?
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=65)** Well, we can write that symbolically as the probability, P, and then in parentheses, of A, and then the vertical line or pipe means given that, or if this other thing is true, and that's B.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=78)** So the probability of A given B is equal to the probability of A and the intersection of B divided by the probability of B.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=88)** But it's much easier to look at this with a picture.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=92)** So let's go to this one.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=93)** We're going to find out what's the probability of randomly drawing a circle, assuming that the thing that you drew was orange.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=101)** Here are the orange circles, and we want to look at the conditional probability.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=107)** So probability of a circle, given its orange, we look at the probability of the intersection of circle and orange.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=114)** That's the three orange circles against the probability of all the orange things.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=119)** So there are four orange things, the intersection of orange and circle, there's three of them.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=127)** And then all orange things is four.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=130)** That's a 75% chance.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=132)** So if you've drawn something that is orange, if you already know that it's orange, there's a 75% chance that it's a circle.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=139)** On the other hand, what if it's blue?
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=141)** Well, in that case, we have our three blue circles right here.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=145)** We use the same formula.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=147)** I've changed some of the symbols, but now, we have our three blue circles compared to 12 blue objects.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=154)** And three divided by 12 is .25 or 25%.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=158)** So, whereas the conditional probability of a circle given that you drew something that was orange was 75%, the conditional probability of something being a circle given that it was blue is only 25%.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=172)** And so you get these significant, meaningful differences depending on the conditions that you are taking as your starting point.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=181)** Now, in terms of real life, these kind of conditional and probability show up in a lot of places.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=186)** For instance, you want to see whether you get a sale given that somebody has chosen something and put it into their shopping cart.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=194)** It's one thing when somebody comes to your website, and they are looking at the things that you have, but if they take the extra step to click on it and put it in the shopping cart, that changes the probability that they will actually purchase the object.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=208)** That's a step further along in the conversion funnel, and you calculate it differently.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=215)** Again, you have to know of all the people who have actually taken the extra step to put it in their shopping cart, how many of those people actually make the purchase, as opposed to how many people who visit your website overall.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=228)** Or thinking about a business.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=230)** The question is, is your business going to succeed?
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=233)** Well, you may have a business plan, you may have a loan.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=236)** You may have some supporters, but really, let's say, for instance, your business has been running successfully for three years.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=244)** What's the probability that it's still going to be running successfully in five years?
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=247)** It's much higher than it is on day one.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=250)** Again, that's a conditional probability, assuming it has already functioned for three years, we can calculate the probability differently.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=257)** Or thinking about movies and whether a movie's going to be hit a flop.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=261)** Well, if it's really successful on its opening weekend, that is a strong, not perfect, but a strong indicator of success over the long run.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=271)** And so what all three of these examples have in common is you're not just trying to calculate the probability out of nothing, but you already have some data.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=280)** You already know that one particular thing has occurred, that a person's put an object in their online shopping cart, that the business has already run successfully for three years, that the movie was successful in its first weekend.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=293)** And then given that piece of information, you can calculate the probability of continued success.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=301)** Moving down the sales funnel, still being in great business in two more years, or having a long run with a film.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/conditional-probability?u=76281980&t=308)** Any one of those can help you get the insights you need to make better decisions, and really give you the actionable insights you need to run your business, your organization, and your life more effectively.

#### Expected values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes when you have to make an important decision, it's helpful to write a list of pros and cons in two separate columns.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=8)** So you can see what the trade offs and the benefits are that are involved.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=12)** Now earlier in this course, I demonstrated a spreadsheet that can help you calculate the value of different choices you might be making.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=20)** Right now however, I'm want to show you a slightly more elaborate approach that has the stamp of approval of economists everywhere.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=27)** The big difference here is that this method explicitly includes the probabilities of different outcomes.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=35)** What we're going to be focusing on is expected values, and here are the steps involved.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=41)** First list, all of the possible outcomes of a decision.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=45)** You want to try to be comprehensive.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=47)** Second, estimate the value of each outcome.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=50)** If you're going to put it in dollars, that's great.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=53)** If you're going to put it in some sort of other utility measure or new customers or happiness involved, that's great, but you need the same metric all the way through.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=62)** And then you estimate the probability of each of these outcomes, and then you multiply the value of each outcome by its probability.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=73)** And then finally, you sum those values, the products, for a single expected value of that decision.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=80)** And then you can compare that to other decisions where you've done the same process, and see which one of them has the highest expected value.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=89)** So here's an example about making your career decision between being an entrepreneur, and starting your own company or getting a salary job with another company.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=97)** Please note that the numbers I'm using here are made up and it may be also that you apply different values to different things and so it can adjust.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=106)** This is simply an example of how the process works.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=109)** Your mileage may vary in terms of the actual numbers, but let's do this.

>

>

>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=310)** And let's give that a probability of 2% 'cuz again, these need to add up to %100 and negative $10,000 times 0.02 is negative $200.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=320)** Well, we sum those.

> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=325)** And $9,600 plus $63,000 minus $200 is $72,400.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=332)** Again, this is made up data, and your estimates may be very different depending on the situation that you're in.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=340)** For the entrepreneur scenario, the expected value across the three outcomes was $56,800 in one year.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=349)** For the salary job, again, based on the numbers that I made up, the expected value was $72,400.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=357)** So based on this, we see that the salary job has an expected value about $16,000 or more compared to the entrepreneurial job.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=367)** Now, that doesn't mean that you necessarily need to make that particular choice.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=370)** The decision has a lot more that goes into it but this is one element of the decision, and it is a way of taking the different kind of outcomes, assigning them a probability, and then using that information to come up with a holistic data-driven decision.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/expected-values?u=76281980&t=389)** Think of it as a way of striking a balance by looking at your alternatives weighing each of them, and finding what work best for you.

#### Sampling variation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] There's a lot to be said for trying something over again and repetition can give you insights into all sorts of things, including data.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=11)** One place where this shows up, is in sampling and sampling variability.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=15)** Now, this is easiest to explain with this little graphical metaphor.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=19)** Here is a population I've shown you of 16 objects.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=23)** And again, imagine that you put them in a hat or a bowl and you are drawing them at random.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=28)** Well, we've got 4 that are orange.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/sampling-variation?u=76281980&t=30)** We've got 12 that are blue.
>

>

#### AI as an additional source of analytical variability
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=0)** You know that in the world around us, nothing is ever truly the same, like these roses.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=6)** They're all flowers and they're all roses, but one is red, the others are pink, and importantly, even the pink ones differ from each other.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=15)** Individuality and variability are inherent elements of the natural world.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=20)** The same thing shows up in terms of randomness in a lot of standard data work.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=26)** So for instance, if you think about random sampling where you are gathering data from a large population, and there is randomness in terms of who you choose, which data points are included in your population.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=40)** If you're doing machine learning, it's a common procedure to partition the data, say for instance, into training and testing sets.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=48)** If you're doing a random forest where you're computing many, many different decision trees, there is randomness in terms of how you split the data up into different forests, how you compose those branches in each one.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=61)** There's a lot of randomness inherent in that particular algorithm.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=65)** And then there's also the issue of cross-validation where you're splitting your training data setup into several different components and then building the model on these components and testing it against that one.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=77)** Now, that's a lot of randomness that can be involved in a lot of things.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=81)** And a common way of dealing with this is to use what's called a random seed.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=87)** And so in a programming language or an application, you can say, use this number to generate the randomness and that makes it repeatable.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=95)** And you need to make sure to include that in your code.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=98)** And that allows you to document it, do the analysis over again and get the exact same results.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=104)** That's an important part of verifying your information.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=108)** Now, AI introduces a whole new level of randomness.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=111)** You may have heard the phrase that AI is a stochastic parrot or stochastic has to do with randomness.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=118)** And the idea is that it is parodying or repeating things that it's heard elsewhere by combining them up in random ways.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=126)** When you're having AI, either generative or agentic AI help you with your analyses, you need to know that for instance, the code that it generates is partially drawn at random from a vast array of resources in its training data.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=142)** And what that means is that code won't replicate from one query to another.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=147)** Somebody else asked the AI to do the same thing, it's probably gonna set it up a little differently.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=152)** You ask it to do it on a different day, it'll probably again set it up a little differently.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=158)** Now, the challenge here is that as far as I know, AI doesn't let you set a random seed.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=164)** You can set a random seed within the code so that if you're generating Python code, it'll execute it the same way.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=170)** But exactly whether it writes the code the same way from one to another is gonna be a different issue.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=175)** So you have an additional source of uncontrolled variability when you use AI to help write your code and to do your analyses.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=184)** So there are a few ways to deal with that kind of randomness.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=188)** First off, you really need to just be aware that it's happening.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=192)** You know that when you're doing a procedure that involves randomness, like a random forest, you know to set the random seed.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=199)** You know to control for that, to make things repeatable.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=202)** Well, you can do similar things with AI.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=205)** So when you get code from your AI, first off, you're going to want to download the code.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=210)** You're going to want to have a written version of it somewhere that you can look at and you can repeat.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=214)** Also make sure that that code has random seeds wherever appropriate.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=218)** So the process itself is repeatable.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=221)** So the AI might generate the code looking a little bit differently, structured differently, but if you have a single source of truth, this is the code, then you can always refer it back to that.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=232)** is going to be very helpful.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=234)** And then finally, when you are interpreting your data, making sense of it and deciding what to do, remember that randomness gets introduced as several different points.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=243)** And remember that randomness can affect both the way you get your results in terms of the random sampling and the random procedures that are involved in the analysis, as well as any assistance you got from the AI, that there is this element of randomization you want to try to nail down, either through random seeds in your code, or creating a single official stable copy of the code that maybe AI generated.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=272)** And then you get that stability.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=274)** But you do need to keep those things in mind that things could work out a little bit differently.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=278)** If you did it differently, that's going to make your interpretations really more valid and more reliable, because you're going to have that variability in mind.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/ai-as-an-additional-source-of-analytical-variability?u=76281980&t=288)** And you can give it a more nuanced and contextual interpretation and application.

>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=109)** We have courses available on all of these that you can use to expand your own learning.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=116)** It might be helpful in your projects to follow the same advice that you'd give to people writing a report or a term paper.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=123)** Write the central question on a sticky note, stick it on the corner of your computer, and make sure that everything you do helps to answer that question.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=132)** In data literacy, this means making sure that your data processing, your visualizations, and your numerical summaries all get you closer to where you want to be.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=143)** Also, you'll want to focus on, for instance, any measurable attribute for prediction, so if you only are trying to predict what's going to happen, you can throw anything you have in there.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=154)** On the other hand, you want to focus on things that are under your control for intervention, so if you're going to design a marketing program to bring about a particular result, well, obviously, the analysis should focus on things that that marketing program can directly affect, what platforms you use, what times you advertise, the way you interact.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=173)** And in all of your projects, you need to keep ROI, or return on investment, clearly in mind so that you're using your time well.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=181)** And then finally, remember that your data comes from people.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=186)** Even if it's something you're getting from a machine, it was something that was developed to get data by people and it's for people, and so having a little bit of empathy and understanding for the people who provided the data you're working with or who were involved in the processing of it can help you better understand how you can use, interpret, and apply the results you get from those analyses.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=212)** But remember, data literacy is for everyone, so don't wait.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/data-literacy-exploring-and-describing-data-in-an-ai-world/next-steps-and-additional-resources-23444787?u=76281980&t=216)** It's time to jump in.
